“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly— Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and if is.
tinctire Austral inn National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System —one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “ nobilitj. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is riot
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin,
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It- had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress; it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the chums of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land,-America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushrnan of the -Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official —that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations thi cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

■i L?U w ..■"■JV/J.
AWARDED TWO GOLD MEDALS, HIGHEST AWARD MELBOURNE CENTENNIAL
INTERNATIONAL EXHIBITION, 1888.
Testing of Watches at the Melbourne Observatory for Awards, Centennial International
Exhibition, 18S8.
The AMERICAN ELGIN NATIONAL WATCH COMPANY scored tho highest number of marks for general
excellence of manufacture, having obtained 866 points out of a possible 400. Rotherhams and Sons obtained 343
points out of a possible 400.
Dec. 21st, 1388. R. L. J. ELLERY, F.R.S., Government Astronomer.
The American Elgin National Watch Company and Messrs. Rotherhams and Sons entered into a Time Test
Competition with a number of their exhibited Watches. The time test was made at the Observatory, where the
watches were under trial for sixteen days in the several positions, and the Elgin Watch Company obtained for time-
U. eping 27 5 marks, against 2P7 of the best Rotherhams and Sons. It is recommended that both exhibits get a First
Award, and the Elgin Watch Company get an extra First-class Award for having obtained the victory in the time tests.
„ ~ ~ , _ „ r L. J. ELLERY, F.R.S., Chairman.”
It must be borne in mind that the Elgin Watchks which obtained the highest number of marks for time-keep'-
inar were of the Sixth Grade (15, W. Raymond), and that.the Rotherhams and Sous' Watches were of their (list nualitv
and had been specially rated and tested at the Kew Observatory, London, before being sent to the Melb. Exhibition. J

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 24, 1894.
A Legal Fiction.
The grand old British law, inherited by
Australia in the primeval days of chains and
darkness, is full of moral precepts, and is
loaded down with much beautiful theory
which is seldom put into practice. There
are few things more lofty and strictly
equitable—in theory—than these statutes of
our British forefathers. They are spotted
; over with axionis which are elevating to
contemplate, and with great principles which
are a liberal education in the doctrine of
justice. One of the noblest of these is
the hackneyed assertion that the law
holds every man to be innocent until he is
proved guilty, and that it not only treats
him accordingly, but requires the general
public to do likewise—so far, at least, as
outward observances are concerned. Its
great object, according to legend and public
conviction, is to secure for every suspected
citizen an absolutely fair trial, and, with this
end in view, it professes to fall with all its
massive weight on anybody who endeavours
to prejudice the minds of the court or the
jury. The newspaper man who, in the
mildest manner, assumes the guilt of the
unconvicted prisoner—at least, when the un-
convicted prisoner is a bank magnate, or
other person of money and influence, and
is able to hire expensive counsel to bring the
matter before the court —gets into gaol with
promptitude and certainty ; though, strange
to say, the one who gratuitously assumes
his innocence does not. What would
happen to the journal which headed its
report of the preliminary proceedings
against a representative citizen with the bald
announcement: “ Arrest of a notorious bank-
thief,” no one dare even imagine. The fact
that the law treats every suspect as an inno-
cent man until his guilt is proved is so well-
known that no paper ever runs such a risk.
That is to say, no paper ever does, unless
the suspect is a man of no importance and
has not the means to hire counsel.
• • *
But when the suspect is a common, vulgar
person, charged with a small, mean offence
—when he isn’t in society, and can’t hire
expensive counsel to fall upon the paper
that is guilty of “ contempt,” and when ho
can’t get bail, and is altogether a suspect of
no account—then the noble axiom above
alluded to utterly falls through. Among the
suspects whoso cases are still sub judice, and
whom the law is especially bound to treat as
innocent men in every way except by letting
them get beyond its jurisdiction, the most
notorious at present .are Matthew Davies
and his colleagues at one end of the social
scale, and at the other the two alleged bur-
glars whose arrest made such a sensation the
other day in Sydney. These latter are charged
with burglary in the first place, and with
assault upon certain constables in the
second. But until their crime is proved by
legal process the law doesn’t know, and pro-
fessedly doesn’t allow anyone else to know,
that they have done anything illegal. It is
as absolutely unlawful for any newspaper to
say that they burgled an office and nearly
murdered five policemen as it would be for
the same paper to say, in so many words, that
Matthew Davies, or anybody else of the
same standing, perpetrated a shameless fraud
on the Mercantile Bank shareholders. In
each case it would be assuming the guilt of
an untried man, and prejudicing the minds
of the jury, and grossly libelling a legally
innocent person, and also committing an
aggravated contempt of court. And no
paper dare do these things in the case of the
“ representative ” banker. But every paper
does them constantly and with absolute im-
punity in the case of the alleged burglar or
any other common offender. The law per-
mits anything to be said about a man who
can’t hire a fifty-guinea counsel to draw its
attention to his wrongs.
• * *
When a person of no wealth or social im-
portance is arrested on a sensational charge,
the course of events is always much the
same. There is an urgent demand among
the daily horror-mongers for his portrait,
and if, as often happens, it can’t be obtained
at short notice, any other brutal-looking
portrait does instead. It matters very little
whose portrait it is so long as it is brutal-
looking. The beetle-brow on this awful
production is, in itself, sufficient to
make a fair trial impossible. The misshapen
ears are a living prejudice, and the vulpine
eyes an epitome of lynch law. The gorilla-
like underjaw is perjured evidence against
the defendant. No editor ventures, by any
chance, to publish the portrait of some
hanged Thug as that of the eminent director
who is suspected of misappropriation, and, if
he did, that law which regards every man as
innocent till he is found guilty would
promptly suppress him. But the other man
can be represented by a picture which carries
murder, arson and cannibalism in every
lineament, and the circumstance creates nob
even a ripple on the Dead Sea of public
opinion.
« * *
In the case of the sensational alleged
burglar the ordinary newspaper report is
equally an outrage upon the nominal priciples
of the law. “ The prisoner was seen issuing
from certain premises. . . . He resisted
violently and felled the officer with a jemmy.
. . . After a furious struggle, in which
two constables were disabled, he was at
length captured. . . . On being searched
at the watch-house a quantity of house-
breaking implements were found in his pos-
session. . . . He has since been recog-
nised as a notorious offender with a long list
of previous convictions,” &c. These are the
sort of statements wi ich are to be found in
any daily paper whenever a suspect of the
ordinary humble type is concerned. Yet,
according to the noble maxims of Anglo Aus-
tralian justice, the law doesn’t know, until
it learns from the verdict of the jury,
whether the defendant was seen coming out
of an office, or out of his own house, or out
of anywhere ; it is totally unaware whether
the officer was brained with a jemmy or
whether he wilfully knocked out his own
intelligence by dashing it against a wall ; it
has no knowledge whether there was any
struggle at all, whether anything was found
on the prisoner, and—unless he announces
his own identity—whether he is a notorious
offender, or merely the Chief Justice who has
been arrested by mistake while out walking
at night in company with the local Bishop or
the Cardinal. All the stereotyped news-
paper assertions are merely the ex parte
statements of the prosecution—that is, of
the police. Therefore, the papers which
publish them as facts simply declare in
advance that the accused is guilty, that the
statements of the prosecution are absolutely
true, that the defence—whatever it may bo
—is a lie, and that it is the bounden duty of
the jury to convict the prisoner, no matter
what evidence he may advance. Yet, if the
same papers accepted, in similar fashion, the
assertions of the prosecution in the case of
the influential bank-director, and stated off-
handedly that he had misused the funds, and
wilfully falsified the balance-sheet, and
obtained deposits under false pretences, and
had been a callous swindler generally—what
would happen ?
• • •
In the case already alluded to the prin-
ciples of equity were outraged in an even
more glaring fashion than usual. On that
occasion the bull-headed Premier Dibbs pro-
ceeded, on the very morning after the event,
to recommend for promotion the officers who
said they had distinguished themselves in the
fray. The promotion was distinctly a reward
for the capture of two dangerous male-
factors. Therefore, by his haste in sending
in the aforesaid recommendation, Dibbs
virtually announced, by actions which speak
more loudly than words, that the accused
were malefactors, that the police charges
brought against them were absolutely true,
and, as the natural consequence, that any
statements they might make in their own
defence, which contradicted these police
assertions, were absolutely false. The pro-
motions in question would have been entirely
unobjectionable after the conviction of the
prisoners—-supposing them to be convicted.
But, as things stood, they ought to have
resulted in Premier Dibrs being hauled
before the Supreme Court, and there fined,
or severely admonished, or committed to
prison for a gross act of contempt and a
constructive interference with the course of
justice. And the same punishment should
have been served out to every editor
who arrogated to himself the rights of the
jury by finding the prisoners guilty without
trial, and thereby proceeded to systematically
create a public feeling which must inevit-
ably make a fair trial impossible.
# # #
The person who assumes, and incites the
public to assume, the guilt of an untried
man, and thereby interferes with the course
of justice to procure his conviction, is
morally just as criminal as the one who
bribes a jury and thereby interferes witli
the course of justice to procure his acquittal.
Also, his offence arises from equally
base and mercenary motives; the “jury-
fixer” does his work for cash payment
from the accused, while the owner of
the sensational newspaper does his for cash
payment derived from the extra circulation
of his daily horror. Further, his offence is
aggravated by the fact that it is never com-
mitted except against a poor and friendless
suspect. The court never interferes in such
cases except when it is officially moved to
do so by an array of high-priced counsel.
Therefore, this gross perversion of justice is
always carefully directed against the prisoner
who is too impecunious to obtain bail, so
that—being in prison and cut off from the
outer world—he has practically no knowledge
of the injury that is being done him ; and
who, even if he should become aware
of it, is too poor to take measures for
his own protection. It is a mean, degrad-
ing, wormy offence which is invariably
directed against the man who is already
defenceless, and never against the one who
is in a position to protect himself. In every
respect the assertion that the law treats every
prisoner as innocent until his guilt is proved
is a lie, so far as any save the wealthy
prisoner is concerned. And, further, it
always has been a lie, and, to all appearance,
it always will be one so long as there are
poor and rich in the land.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Chessplay for Seaboards.
The combinations on the European chessboard
are developing startling variations. It is com-
monplace comment to assert that the latest
tariff reciprocity between Germany and Russia
is a Bismarckian move intended to isolate
France, but there are far deeper significances
behind. A counter-move, mysteriously inspired,
is an effort at commercial rapport between
Italy and France. The commonplace observa-
tion on that is that the Triple Alliance of
Germany, Austria, and Italy seems to be un-
stranding. But all these matters are a part of
the great grabs that are being made at seaboard.
Austria warred with Italy for seaboard and
annexed some Adriatic provinces. The Crimean
war was, in reality, not a dispute as to churches,
but one in which Russia acquisitively desired
an always open outlet tor her fleets into the
Mediterranean. So with the Russo-Turkish
war. Germany bought Heligoland from Britain
in order that the Baltic might be her own
free sea, but the Baltic is not all that Germany
desires, since it is easily blockaded, even with-
out Heligoland. Germany has acquired a
diplomatic suzerainty over Holland, because of
the latter’s grand seaboard. Some day Holland
w’ill be a German State, if the fates be propi-
tious. It was Germany that really put forward
Holland’s claim to Dutch New Guinea. British
statesmen have fallen into the trap of German
alliances blindfold. Barkis Bismarck was
willing. Barkis Bismarck was so willing that
he entered into friendly rivalry with J. Bull
in the colonising areas of the world, and
the two States have advanced side by
side and stride for stride in Africa. And
Germany and Britain are the two great rivals
for the supremacy of the Pacific. Russia, on
the other hand, has bluntly been Britain’s foe,
and endeavoured to gain seaboard somewhere
or other by force of arms. Failing the Bos-
phorus, it wrenched Vladivostock from China.
Moving unfalteringly towards Afghanistan, it
means to try for an Indian Ocean seaboard.
Bailing that, it can branch off into the Persian
Gulf. Seaboard it will have. If Britain and
other European Powers let Russia take Con-
stantinople, the Indian aggressiveness would be
abandoned. The friendliness between Germany
and Russia may have this chess-ending : “ You
help me in my designs on Holland, and I’ll
help you auent Constantinople. Let Austria
wilt.” Italy’s move towards France is a clear
case of hedging, in view of possible danger.
The real key to the whole problem is the
isolation, not of France, but of Britain, Yet,
it may bo that France, of all seeming present
probabilities, will once more be the ally of
“perfidious Albion,” It is Germany that is
Britain’s unsleeping enemy.

Patterson’s Land of Promise,
The windy Ministry of Patterson continues
to witch Victoria with its settled policy of
puff and blow, and the things which it was
going to do, and didn’t, promise to outnumber
the hairs on its chieftain’s scalp. From the
time when Patterson and Carter undertook
to save the banks by “cordial co-operation”
the history of this remarkable Government has
been that of an impotent volcano, ever smoking
and spluttering and rumbling ominously, but
throwing up naught save a little dust and
ashes. Patterson was going to construct a
“ necessary' railway,” or two, and didn’t. He
talked of importing an American railway-
expert ami dropped -the idea directly it began
to bum his lingers. He was making arrange-
ments to carry on the Agent-General’s office
without an A.G. when suddenly Giddies was
inserted into the empty nest. He swore
not to let Davies leave for Colombo, but
Davies enjoyed his trip all the same.
The despatch of a beatified butterruan
to foreign parts was said to be on the verge of
occurring, and lo ! Patterson decided not to
send the butterman at all. His invitation to
the Yorks was a mere gilding of the dome of the
Invalides—a mere pretence of doing something.
Adapting himself to the new meat mania he
proposed to erect Government freezing works
in Melbourne, and so “ devclope a great in-
dustry,” but he now truly slates that private
independent action is the one thing necessary
for the meat trade. All round the country he
rants and roars over the free banquet-table
his intention to put the finances straight, the
while that he knows they are getting daily
more crojik, and he keeps Parliament shut up to
prevent the possibility of a land or property
tax doing any damage to his precious
“popularity,” Patterson’s declaration of
sympathy for James Munro, and his avowed
scorn for the “persecutors” of Matthew
Davies, and t he hanging of Mrs. Knorr, are
probably the only things that he has stuck to
hard and fast since he started to save Victoria,
and this is a very solemn matter for considera-
tion at the present time. Premier Patterson
is bent on seeing that nobody wants for food
during the coming winter. His policy for the
moment is centred upon the task of taking care
that the pangs of hunger and cold shall be
unknown among his loyal subjects. Wherefore
by all rules of precedent it appears certain that
hundreds of people in Victoria shall presently
moan for food, clothes and tiring, inasmuch as
Patterson promises to the contrary.
Confidence Men.
By latest advices there is to be more flogging in
New South Wales. The additional crimes to
which the good old nostrum is to be applied-
provided Mr. E. M. Clark’s bill becomes law
are many, the list including :
Burglary, house-breaking, robbery from the person with
and without striking or wounding, wounding with intent
to do bodily harm or resist arrest, assaults with intent
to commit felony or on certain officers, assaults and dis-
turbances by pushes, picking pockets, and the offence
ordinarily known as the confidence'trick.
Therefore the Legislature will presently have
to sit down in a cool place and think out a
comprehensive definition of a “push” and a
“ confidence trick,” and if it is successful —
especially so far as the latter term is concerned
—it will do a good and great work, and hand
down a name which will be reverenced by
posterity. For the “ confidence trick ”is
a crime which wants to be put down
—by flogging dr otherwise worse than
any other in Australia. In a general
sense it covers any variety of swindle which
consists, first, in inspiring confidence, and then
in abusing it and leaving the confiding one
penniless, or as nearly so as possible. The suave
The Suicidal "Stigma."
A number of British divines; have signed
a petition to the Canterbury’ Convocation,
urging the Church to use its influence
upon coroners’ juries for the prevention of
suicides. Suicides have of late increased at
’Ome, owing to the rapid growth of misery, and
the petitioners (who humbly pray) consider that
the practice of finding a verdict of temporary
insanity ought to be abandoned. The good old
“ stigma ” attaching to cases of feto de se was a
Christian deterrent to self-destruction, say
these servants of God, wherefore they want to
see the “stigma” in full blast as of yore.
Many a man who now wipes himself out, upon
the assumption that he will pass muster as a
lunatic, would rather die naturally than have
his cold remains convicted of a crime with a
stigma to it. As regards the restraining custom
of cross-roads burial and fixing the corpse down
with an iron skewer, the British clergymen say
nothing straight out, though they hint at its
efficacy.—
O, restore the lost stigma attaching
To suicide once on a time,
When deeds of self-happy-despatching
Found punishment fitting the crime—
When men, with their brains in a ferment,
Made sober would speedily be
By a dread of the lonely interment
Awaiting a felo de se.
Then the ruffian bent on expiring
Would gaze at the knife in his paw,
Or blunderbuss loaded for firing,
And pause to consider the Law. {
“My word !” he exclaimed with a ■ lover,-'
“ A hole in four roads they will delve, ■
And drive a stake into my liver,
Poor deader ! exactly at twelve.”
He hacked at his throat, feeling sure he
Would go to the “ other place ” when *
The foreman who spoke for thb jury
Had d-—d him forever, Amen.
No prayers o’er his carcase weye chanted,!
No blessing was shed by a priest; ;
As a felo de se he was planted i
Away from his fellow deceas’t.
Say, why did wo part with they stigma,
The stigma druv in by the stake?
Can Charity solve this enigma,
Or Reason explain the mistake?
From the scorn of the world We exempt him,
The wretch who was certainly sane,
And thus do our hardest to tempt him s
To kill himself over again. <,
Small wonder that clergy are rising
Against this unjustified ruth !
No corpse should escape front chastising
’Through jury-perversion oft truth.
The sons of misfortune and daughters
Of shame seek nepenthe when sad ;
But only a parson who slaughters
Himself is undoubtedly mad.


individual who induces a simple-minded person
to let him hold his money, and tow r ait while he
walks round the corner and fails to come back,
is of this order. So is the influential bank-
director who, by his eloquent speeches at the
annual meeting, induces some other “ mug ” to
let the bank hold his money while it goes round
the corner on no visible security, and doesn’t
come back. The eminently pious church-
member of awful and cast-iron morality will be
floggable under this heading when he breaks up
and pays a few coppers in the £ ; the piety and
morality and the church-membership inspire
the confidence, and the subsequent burst is the
confidence-triek. The person who does a dis-
graceful swindle after for years publicly wear-
ing a shiny bell-topper and a white waistcoat
and a shaven upper-lip and a prayer-book, will
also come under the provisions of the Bill—the
bell-topper and the waistcoat are exhibited in
order to inspire confidence, just as the bogus
notes of the ordinary spieler are exhibited to
the person from the country ; the process is the
same and has the same ending. The person
whose K.C.M.G.-ship, or whose teetotalism, or
whose sympathy for the poor, misguided
heathen, or whose loyalism, or aggressive
seriousness and respectability are used to
inspire confidence, which confidence afterwards
proves to be hideously misplaced, will all
be duly skinned under this Act. It is
an Act for the castigation of bank-managers
and directors, fraudulent trustees, solicitors of
high standing, Members of Parliament, pillars
of the church, people who wear long hats and
pompous vests and who live in big stone
mansions—in short, for the castigation of the
Fat Man in general. All of which, of course,
is based on the assumption that it is interpreted
on an equitable principle which will put the
big confidence-man, who swindles in a gorgeous
office with tesselated floors and adornments of
malachite, on the same level as the small one
who swindles in the back of a fusty public-
house amid the effluvium of stale beer and old
sodden cigar stumps. But probably it will
prove in the end that it is only the latter that
Clark’s Bill is looking for, and that the
confidence-man in the massive frock-coat—for
instance, the elaborately-tailored saint who
sells, on the instalment plan, to some poor, trust-
ful ignoramus, a section of land of which he, the
vendor, has hopelessly mortgaged the deeds—
isn’t even taken into consideration.
M’Kiuuon’s Motion.
The following resolution, moved last week in
N.S.W. Assembly by M’Kinnon, M.L.A., is a
premonitory symptom of coming trouble for the
aggregate Fat Man of Australia
That in the opinion of this House the Government
should at once appoint auditors to examine and report
upon the financial position of all hanks which have availed
themselves of tire Bank Issue Act.
There is one obvious mistake in the drafting of
this resolution—it should have applied, not
only to institutions which availed themselves
of the Bank Issue Act, but to banks in general.
Also, it should cover insurance societies, and,
in short, all companies which are, entrusted
with the money of the public. The debate on
Mr. M‘Kinnon’s motion covered most of the
arguments on both sides of the question.
The case for the Lean Man—the represen-
tative of the great, aggregate, long-suffering,
much-swindled depositor, was practically this :
The private auditor is a delusion. He is
generally appointed by the directors and their
adherents, and if he goes habitually seeing
things he isn’t wanted to see he speedily loses
his billet. He is miserably underpaid—so
miserably that if he devoted sufficient time to
the accounts of a big financial concern to audit
them thoroughly, he would only earn some-
thing lik£2os. per week. And he is altogether
a mere automaton, as is demonstrated by the
fact that every rotten and utterly dishonest
financial institution which has ever collapsed
under disgraceful circumstances, in Australia,
was audited and solemnly declared, by allegedly
responsible men, to be sound right up to the
day of its decease. And, on the other
side of the question, the case for the
Fat Person, who strongly objects to being
inquired into by any process of State audit, was
expressed by Messrs. See and Wall, who rose
to state jointly that “ the majority of the banks
were thoroughly sound,” and that “ the share-
holders were sure to see that the affairs of the
bank were properly audited.” Considering the
record of the Federal, Mercantile, Commercial
ofS.A., the Real Estate Bank, the Imperial,
Anglo-Australian, V.D.L., the famous Abigail
institution, the Freehold Investment horror,
and a good many dozen others which died sim-
ply because the shareholders didn’t, and
couldn’t, and never can know whether the
accounts are properly audited or not, this latter
statement is about as brassy as anything well
can be. The one fact which sticks out
conspicuously like an accusing skeleton
out of a sepulchre, is that the ordinary
shareholder or depositor can’t, by any pos-
sibility. know whether the accounts are
properly audited or not, because he only
knows what the directors and the auditors tell
him, and they always tell him the same thing,
and the thing that they tell him is more often a
he than otherwise. As for the statement that
the majority of the surviving banks are
thoroughly sound, it rests on exactly the same
authority as the former statements that all the
dead banks were thoroughly sound till they
died, and it is advanced by much the same
crowd, and supported by the same evidence. If
the banks and the big insurance societies and
building societies and all the rest are sound,
they should be glad to have the fact demon-
strated by State inspection, and the apparent
resolution of their friends to die in the last
ditch rather than have it demonstrated is
eminently suspicious. It is possible for the
State to thoroughly audit the accounts of busi-
ness corporations. See how the United States
audits its life-assurance companies.
One of the papers tells a pathetic story of an
Australian speculative Christian. A good
many years ago a land “ shindykit,” composed
mainly of solid citizens of good religious stand-
ing, bought an “estate” and cut it up into
allotments, which were sold on the instalment
plan at several hundreds per cent, profit. One
of the purchasers was a mechanic who wanted
to make a home for himself and his family. He
bought his allotment, and for years and years,
in good seasons and bad, he persistently
scraped up the money for the monthly instal-
ment. The land-boom died - a natural death ;
and was succeeded by years of flood, and lean-
ness, and scarcity of work, and low wages, and
general desolation, but still the buyer struggled
through, and when his last instalment was
paid he asked for his deeds. Then he was
referred to a bank, where he learned that the
estate had been long , since mortgaged up
to the hair, and foreclosed upon, and, in
short, his allotment was gone, along with
many other allotments sold on the same
fraudulent principle. There was no redress
save by starting a huge equity suit against the
bank and the syndicate, and engaging an
eminent firm of solicitors, and hiring three
barristers of repute and two juniors, and paying
jury fees, and court fees, and shorthand-writer’s
fees, and costs out of pocket, and so on, and
then carrying an appeal to the Privy Council
where it would have been heard after two
years or so—consequently the home and the
result of years of toil had to go. But
now the local council has got a verdict
against the defrauded buyer for accumulated
rates on the stolen allotment—the syndicate
having allowed the rates to get into arrear and
being now dissolved and unfindable ; and the
said rates are recoverable by “distress”-—in
other words, by seizure of the unhappy pur-
chaser’s tables and chairs and his bed, all of
which are probably gone by this time. Truly,
the poor man’s thrift is the fat man’s oppor-
tunity, and the fat man advocates thrift princi-
pally because he lives thereon. Also he objects
to cheap law because it is dear law which keeps
him out of gaol.
Dear Bulletin, —“Titus Salt” has so com-
pletely anticipated the arguments with which
I had intended to confront “ Pax ” that I will
herewith only furnish some collateral evidence.
1. It is the “ emasculated ” nation that most
delights in the combat of brutes—human or
otherwise. The most masculine men the world
has ever seen—the iron Romans of the Repub-
lic—knew nothing of the “ gladiatorics ” which
increased in vogue exactly as. the national
emasculation progressed. 2. General Grant
said, “I do not consider the pugilist a really
courageous man, and I would rather take a
regiment of quill-drivers into action than a
regiment of pugilists. Within certain accus-
tomed lines, the fist-fighter is well enough—
outside them he is a brute, and a poor one
at that!” 3. Lieut.-Col. Stuart says, “It
is a curious fact, observed by not a few
commanding officers, that the men who are un-
steady in action are generally men who have a
pugilistic reputation in their regiments.” 4.
Jackson (not Peter, but “ Gentleman-JACK-
son ” of the Twenties) “ I have kept it”
(pugilism) “as decent as it could be kept, but
the best proof of its real character as a profes-
sion is that I, as a member of it, should be held
up as a miracle, for merely being a tolerably
respectable member of the community.” 5. Is
it the part of a “Spartan” to live, as many of
the obscurer pugilists do, upon the earnings of
prostitutes, of whom their professional “man-
liness ” does not forbid them to make punching-
pads on the slightest provocation?—A.
Dear Bulletin,—Y our correspondent who asks
(Feb. 10) how the exclusion of children under
six from N.S. Wales State schools will affect
the education of the next crop of adults, as
compared with those of Victoria and Queens-
land, may be answered that the country will save
much and the children will lose nothing. It is
incorrect to say that in Victoria and Queensland
children are taken at three years. The school
age is supposed to be five years in each pro-
vince, though children are sometimes sent
before, as the teacher cannot refuse to accept
the parent’s statement of age. I believe
teachers generally will agree that until six or
seven years a child is best at home with its
mother. To send children to school before six,
if they really learn anything, is to risk lower-
ing their vitality and degrading their physique,
which, at that age, should be the first considera-
tion. Many mothers have been in the habit of
thinking that the State should supply nursery
governesses to mind their infants while they
tidied the house or went shopping. Minister
Suttor is right in destroying this amiable
delusion.—Yours, Alpha.
The Separation journals of the North are
writhing in a spasm of delight, for the Lord
hath delivered their arch-enemy, the Brisbane
Courier, into their hands. In attempting to
show that the financial position of Queensland
for 1893 is sound, that organ delivered an elo-
quent piece of special pleading for Separation.
The Courier first lays down the proposition
that out of its gross exports the province first
pays for its imports, and then from the balance
pays the interest on outside debt, public and
private—the remainder, if there be any, being
profit. Now, the excess of exports over imports
for three years (1891-93) was ten and a-half
millions, of which £5,400,000 was contributed
by the Centre ; £5,104,000 by the North ; and
£BB,OOO by the South. The interest on debt
(public and private) for the same period was
also £10,500,000, of which the South paid next
to nothing- according to the Courier’s own
showing. Again, the local public debt of the
province (railways, harbours, etc.) is about
£26,000,000, of which £15,000,000 were spent
in the South ; £5,000,000 in the Centre ; and
£6,000,000 in the North. On this basis the
interest of the debt for three years should be
thus allotted : South, £6,000,000 ; Centre,
£2,000,000; North about £2,500,000 whereas
the South (vide Courier) gets off with £BB,OOO,
while the North and Centre each pays about
£5,000,000 apiece into the pocket of the foreign
usurer. No wonder the South doesn’t want
Separation. No wonder the political mention
of Philp’s name in Townsville is usually accom-
panied by a frantic howl for tar and feathers
and a substantial rail from the nearest available
fence I

Two minutes’ reflection will surely show that
the shearers are right in refusing to give to or
take references from their employers. Their
case is not that of domestic servants, or of
seamen, instanced as analogous. Neither
seamen nor domestic servants have to deal with
an organised body of employers, acting from
the same motives, influenced by the same ideas,
seeking the same ends. Ship master and house-
mistress have no guide but the written reference
in choosing a sailor or a servant from the mass
of floating labour. The pastoralists have their
union, which is naturally in perpetual conflict,
patent or covert, with the shearers’ union. A
union shearer who bears a reference from one
union squatter to another plainly lights the torch
to his own destruction. Whether a system
of private marks exists or not, it is easy to
conceive that the squatter, falling as far short
of perfection as other people, may through the
reference wreak a union grudge. To be sure,
abolition of references will not prevent this,
since it is the easiest thing in the world to
create a pastoralists’ black-list, absolutely de-
barring from employment those whose names
are included. But this will involve report to
the pastoralist management, who may be trusted
to exercise some oversight, some sense of fitness
and justice. When the reference goes direct
from squatter to squatter, the shearer is lost
utterly. Even a jury of pastoralists is better
than no jury at all. If there must be a black-list,
the official list is infinitely preferable to the
private list created by the reference system.
Further, supposing that every squatter is an
angel of justice and mercy, and that the snake
of individual malice will never, never glide into
the Eden of references, the references are still
misleading and worthless. For there is no ulti-
mate criterion of merit. Some squatters like a
fast shearer, and some a slow shearer ; some
prefer one style of cutting, and some another
style; some have this standard of behaviour,
and some a standard entirely different. Of
what use can a reference from one of these
antagonistic sets of employers be to a man who
seeks employment from one of the other set ?
Happily there is no reason why conflict should
follow the shearers’ decision. After all, the
presence or absence of a reference does not affect
the character of the man. Every shearer was
at one time without a reference, and next season
will simply see all without —or all unionists.
Probably the squatters will prefer men with
references ; but if the shearers hold firm this
need make little difference to them. As for the
resolution not to engage through the pastoralists’
office, it appears that this practice is not
universal at present, and that shearers may
either adopt it or not. If they say “No!” on
the ground that they wish to choose their sheds,
and dislike to be marched about like puppets,
their action cannot be blamed, their right can-
not be denied.
The middleman again ! In common with all
the wheat-growing countries of the world Vic-
toria’s wheat-crop this year is excessive, and
good milling wheat can, as this is written, be
bought in Melbourne at 2s. sd. per bushel, or
lid. less than the ruling prices of 1893.
Although this should mean that the Victorian
consumer will save over a quarter of a million
on his bread bill, it is highly improbable that
this cheerful prospect will be realised when the
treatment experienced by the producer is ex-
plained. One firm secured the tonnage of
nearly all outgoing vessels as far back as Sep-
tember last, and those’producers who stand
outside the ring will have to pay at least 30s.
freight per 480 lbs. for all surplus stocks
which, iu the usual course of trade, are
exported to France. This (freight) charge must
inevitably preclude all chances of recouping the
cost of production when it is remembered that
the Chamber of Deputies has decided to impose
an import duty. The moral for the grower is
obvious. Let him limit his output to the needs
of his country, and abandon his Freetrade
notions of depending for subsistence on external
resources. The consumer, realising that he
seldom gets the benefit of local competition, can
concentrate his hungry optic on the schemes of
domestic middlemen with less unsatisfactory
results than would characterise his attempted
supervision of foreign wire-pullers on the outer
rim of the earth’s surface.
Canon Sharp (to the boys at King’s Schoob
Parramatta, N.S.W.) :
When we set aside and think not of the fear and the
love of God —when we allow large sections of our people
to sink back into virtual paganism—what wonder is it
that we should have amongst us an epidemic of fraud ?
What wonder that our public life should he degraded?
What wonder that reckless gambling and drunkenness
and impurity and larrikinism that fears not God neither
regards man should he rampant among us?
And, now, can Canon Sharp explain how it is
that it is not the backslider into paganism at all,
but the greasy saint, who affects both to fear
God and to regard man, who is the germ of the
“epidemic of fraud”—who also is the “im-
pure ” beast that, under the cloak of sanctity,
ravens on the innocence of the lambs of the
fold? It is the man who boasts that his heart
is fixed in “ the fear and the love of God ” who,
somehow or other, pulls off all the double
events of Satanism that Canon Sharp ascribes
to “virtual paganism.” That is why Canon
Sharp’s homily is just so much wasted breath
—it misplaces its denunciation.
Duncan Gillies, ere departing for his British
picnic, had the astounding gall to debit the
sins of Victoria to Australia at large. This is
commonly done by the “representative” gang
whose friends have “made Smellbourne what
it is,” but never before had the deed been per-
petrated with as much publicity and impudence.
The little oily man placed the continent under
his patronage thuswise : “A great deal that
has been said of these colonies has been said
under a misapprehension. (Cheers.) I am
confident that a great deal that has been said of
these colonies to their detriment has been said
wrongfully and unjustly, and that the best men
in the old country, those who think most of
these colonies and know most about them, will
be prepared to acknowledge that they have no
reason to doubt their honour. (Loud and pro-
longed cheering.) So far as I shall come
in contact with men of influence in the
old country, I shall always speak frankly to
them about this country.” If Duncan carries
out his promise to the letter he will frankly in-
form all and sundry that Victoria is the financial
plague-spot of Australia. And the informa-
tion will be unnecessary because the Britishers
“who know most about these colonies” are
already posted up in the facts of the scandal.
It is Victoria, and Victoria only, that stinks of
fraud, corruption, and compositions, in the
place where puny-minded Duncan will (D.V.)
soon find himself. The laws, manners, and
customs of Smellbourne are pretty well under-
stood over there, and if Duncan starts to
apologise for any province but his own it will
be the duty of Saul Samuel and brethren to
bring him back to the point, or perish in the
attempt.
The best apology for alcohol extant in a popular
form is contained in a sermon lately preached by
Minister Gibson, of Carnoustie, Scotland, from
the text, “ Wine that maketh glad the heart of
man.”—(Psalms civ., 15). He said :
The fanatical teetotaller makes a great mistake in trj'-
ing to abolish the drink traffic. Drink is a creation of
God, intended for the good of man. Alcohol has done
more good than evil in the world. For one that is killed
by it there are 100 gladdened and helped. As opium,
temperately used, is a blessing to the ryot of India, so
beer is a boon to the workman of Britain. Take it from
him, and you deprive him of one of the few solaces of his
hard existence. It is a tonic, a stimulant, and a grateful
vegetable beverage to him. Kind nature grows the barky
and the hops for the joys and the feeding of
her toil-worn children. The wine of Europe is
given by the Great Miracle-worker to cheer the hearts
of the peasantry. It makes their treadmill life possible;
nay, it renders government possible. The rabid tee-
totaller would withdraw it from their lips, but God is
more merciful. Every autumn He sends it gushing in red
rivers out of His beneficent bosom to feed and gladden
the people. When God comes to earth He Himself drinks
it, and so sanctifies its use through all time. The
strongest and greatest races of the world use it. All
great writers, thinkers, preachers, and workers, with few
exceptions, are moderate drinkers. The nations that
don’t drink are lagging in the rear of humanity. The
Mahommedan is teetotal, but he is given to opium
and sensuality. The Turk is sober, but he is the
sick man of Europe, sensual too, retrograde, and
almost moribund. He has banished the God-
planted vine from the Holy Land, and with it
progress and virility. The result of his doings there is
teetotalisra and an intellectual and moral desert. Drink
slays its thousands, no doubt, but it is not the chief sin of
our country. Avarice, lust, dishonesty, ruin more men
spiritually than drink. It is given for use and not abuse.
It goes without saying that it should not be used by
adolescents and youths. Some people are abstainers by
instinct; others wisely abstain when they see they cannot
stop in time. There is again the man whose abstinence is
due to some sad bit of family history, to the injury or
ruin through drink of some near relative. The motive of
the latter is touching and honourable. Let each be fully
persuaded in his own mind ; hut let all be temperate.
Among the painful cacophony of State wails,
the wail of the school-teacher has just now
unusual prominence. Not to put too fine a
point upon it, the school-teacher wants more
screw. There are many admirable reasons why
he (she) should get more screw, and there is
only one why she (he) shouldn’t: the country
can’t afford the money. That reason is, unfor-
tunately, conclusive ; and this is why. The
State school-teacher plays the most important
part in the most important department of State
labour. While most of us are chattering in the
present, he (she) is steadily building up the
future. Neither money nor pains should be
spared to secure her (his) efficiency. Obviously,
at the ordinary market-rate of remuneration for
such things, you cannot expect all the arts and
sciences for £IOO a year. Like every other
employer, to get a good man (or woman), the
State must pay good money. But (1) the State
doesn’t w T ant all the arts and sciences for £IOO
a year ; it only wants a certain specified amount
of learning and capacity, and affirms that this
amount is purchasable at current rates. (2) Many
teachers are paid more than £IOO a year, and
if the avenues to promotion were only kept
open, if aged and incompetent teachers were
removed from well-salaried positions, there
would be far less ground for complaint in the
lower grades. And (3) unfortunately—and this
is a strong point—the supply of the State
school teacher is greater than the demand.
There are more of him (and far more of her)
than the State needs. The calling is not
laborious, the working-days are only five a
week, and the hours are short; there are eight
solid weeks of paid holidays in the school
year ; the position is secure and involves little
anxiety; railway-fares are reduced or abolished,
even if the accommodation is only second-
class. All these things make State school-
teachership sought after, and they must be taken
into account when considering the salary which
they inevitably tend to decrease. The State
cannot disregard them, cannot afford to dis-
regard them. There is a constant pressure on
the Education Department, as on all other
State departments, by young men (and women)
who deliberately prefer the greater ease and
security of the Civil Service to the greater risks
and profits of private business. They cannot
fairly expect to combine State privilege with
private profits. State administration, to be
successful, must look first to the public interest,
and only secondarily to the interest of its ever-
growing class of officials. There remains in
some minds an impression that the State exists
for the benefit of its servants, and not its ser-
vants for the benefit of the State. The sooner
that impression is removed, the better.

One day James Tyson, bachelor and cattle-
king, Australia’s rictiest man, on his way to one
of his out-stations, was caught in a heavy night-
storm and called at a neighbour’s, where he made
himself known, asked to be shown to the bachelors’
quarters—and was made comfortable in the house.
When the hostess asked him why ho had not
visited them sooner, seeing that he lived compara-
tively close, James replied: “Well, you see, I
thought you kept up too much style for me, but I
shall come again, for there’s no style about you.”
The lady, who tells the story herself, still wonders
whether or not the long millionaire intended a
compliment.

The “ Firm’s ” big spectacular drama at
Sydney Lyceum still blazes with unabated splen-
dour down the corridors of Time. There is nothing
especially appropriate about this statement, but
the vastaess of the occasion calls for large and in-
coherent observations. The piece has been com-
pressed somewhat since the opening night; in order
to bring it within a reasonable compass, and,
consequently, the dialogue has suffered a little.
There was exceedingly little dialogue even at the
beginning, and what there was wouldn’t have
been missed, so this abbreviation doesn’t matter
much. Also, the 800 tons or so of scenery goes
smoothly now, and without a hitch. On the
opening night The Bulletin, in an unwonted
spirit of consideration for the vastuess of the
enterprise, forebore to mention that the ship
Henrietta was unmanageable, and threatened more
than once to sink with all hands, and that the
road-agents talked with such needless persistence,
while waiting for the train, as to suggest that the
train had stuck somewhere, and was being shoved
desperately by the whole force of the company.
These troubles, however, have been surmounted.
The axle-box of the wilderness has been greased
to make it go smoothly, and the heaving ocean has
got its second wind, and the primeval forest has
evidently straightened up and sworn off drink.
* • »
As for the company, The Bulletin is in-
clined to the opinion that Miss Polly Emery will
gather more new laurels than anybody else. Miss
Polly is about the best slavey on the Australian
stage, and her wild pursuit of certain domestic
duties under difficulties is a pleasant thing to con-
template. Caicedo, the Columbian, now turns an
extra somersault on the wire, and when his two
rather brief legs and his gleaming spurs and his
shiny top-boots are in the air the public enthu-
siasm is boundless. When he clangs the spurs
together in his atmospheric flight the enthusiasm
is a little more boundless still. A certain lavish
development about the hinder expanse of Caicedo
makes him look a trifle less graceful when he is
upside-down than he does at other times, but he is
always graceful, more or less.
** * *
The early enthusiasm over ‘ ‘ True as
Steel,” at Sydney Majesty’s, has faded somewhat
within the last few days, and that drama will
retire accordingly. The Bulletin, for one, will
regret its departure. The big mill-wheel in par-
ticular was a valuable moral lesson which no Sun-
day-school infant should have missed. It was
round and endless, like eternity. When it started
to grind up George Rignold and the half-drowned
girl in its revolutions it was the very epitome of
Necessity which goes on for ever, and rolls over
every thing in its road, and never looks back to see
if it is a king or a dog that has been squashed.
It was suggestive of the mills of God which grind
slowly, but which grind exceedingly small,
unless the funny man appears at the window
above at the correct moment, and lifts you
off the mill, and leave it to grind away
without you. And the greenness and slimi-
ness of it, and its general crackedness, and
its aspect of old age and decay were evidently
meant to show that even a feeble instrument can
fill a great emergency when the unseen hand of
Providence is shoving it around. The Bulletin
will be sorry to lose that wheel. Also, it has
waited in pained expectancy to see if the steam-
hammer wouldn’t colne down at last on the
beautiful Roberts’ head, but it never came, and
unless it does it by Friday night the chance is
gone. “ The Shamrock and the Rose,” a drama
new to Australia, goes up on Saturday.

“Captain Fritz”is expected to fill Sydney
Criterion on Saturday, and will do its best to
that end. “Fritz” is described as a romantic
musical comedy, and it is an awful fact that the
(•oinantic comedy is generally maudlin, and the
romantic musical comedy is usually a species of
updres,sed farce with wails. If the new produc-
tion is none of these things then it should open
with bright prospects.
* * * 4k
The old Royal remains shut, and its future
continues to be uncertain. Sydney, in the present
stage of its progress through a vale of tears, can’t
support four theatres all at once, therefore the
Royal is waiting for some new developments. If
the Cri. is struck by lightning, or the Firm is
fatally gored by a wild gentleman cow, and its
big house shut up, then the ancient establishment
will rise once more to the occasion.
** * *
The return of Nellie Stewart to Melb.
Princess’s was celebrated in a fitting manner last
Saturday night. Scaffoldings covered with out
flowers and green stuff found their way to the
stage at critical moments, whereupon the cheers
of the public increased in violence, for nothing
stimulates enthusiasm like an unwieldy floral
tribute. Encores were showered upon the lady
(and truly she deserved them) as a matter of
course. Altogether the occasion vied in splendour
with that first return of Nellie Stewart from Eng-
land, some years since, when, also, she made her
bow as Dorothy Bantam. All other principals in
the cast had changed—Fanny Liddiard, Wood-
field, Knight Aston, Federici, Elton, Alice
Barnett and Ida Osborne were not there—but
Miss Nellie obliterated the lapse of time, so to
speak, and brought the treasured past right up to
date. It was generally conceded on Saturday that
the prima donna’s artificialities of gesture were
less pronounced than when she played at the
Opera House in ’9l, and that her voice was
rounder and sweeter than the one she brought to
bear upon her first “ Dorothy ” performance. So
this third coming of Nellie may be written down
a genuine success. The opera is charmingly
sung all round, and some of the concerted num-
bers go distinctly better than ever. The early
trio between Misses Nellie Stewart, Florence
Voung, and May Pollard, the quartette—“Swear
to be good and true ” —and other familiar items
are heard with fresh interest. Tapley’s opening
solo, terminating with a flight into falsetto, is a
tasteful bit of work. The story of Dorothy seems
very thin and old now, but Lauri’s dancing and
acrobatics as Lurcher don’t leave much Elton to
he desired, and the acting in general is not un-
worthy of the siiiging. “Dorothy” runs six
nights only. “The Mountebanks ”to follow.
** * *
At Melb. Alexandra they had the painful
satisfaction of turning money away from all parts
on Saturday night. There are many windows
adjoining the roof of the oval-shaped edifice, but
the gods having ordained that these windows shall
be closed in winter, they remain shut in summer,
and the recent popularity of the Haleck has its
drawbacks when the weather grows sultry. With
more air let into the proceedings, “After Dark”
will go on and prosper, for it is an intellectual
feast as compared with previous products of
Dan Barry’s season. The original drama has
been more or less pulled about to suit this
occasion, and its strong scenic points stick
out less obtrusively as of yore nevertheless,
the spirit of Dion Boucicault governs the show.
Miss Lilia Wilde is a useful addition to the com-
pany, while Miss Dora Moatyn gets another
chance to show her aptitude for ragged boy parts
and chip in a pleasing ballad. Dan Barry might
roar louder and gnash his teeth more violently as
Old Tom, yet his performance is respectable, like
Appleton’s in the r6le of a black-wigged convict
from Australia. Furthermore, Martyn Hagan, in
the music-hall scene, is a cheerful break in the
clouds of sin and suffering, and the accessories are
all that can be expected, or even more.

Melbourne Royal is to be re-opened on
Saturday by Maggie Moore in her managerial
capacity of Mrs. Williamson, Tempting prices,
from 3s. downwards, are advertised, and bonnets
will receive a welcome to the dress-circle, as in
America, where the taint of vice-regalism does
not infect Democracy. For the sake of this inno-
vation, apart from all other facts, the Maggie
Moore season deserves to prove a huge success.
The Mascotte artist and company will lead off
with “The Golden Giant,” and play other cards
when they are called for.

Funny Fred Garnett is just now appear-
ing at the Alhambra, where things generally are
much the same as usual. The Cycloramas and
Aquariums announce themselves to the world
with increasing confidence since show business
began to improve all round, also the Paltner-Beau-
mont company, just back from a long Maoriland
campaign, will try the effect of a concert at the
Exhibition on Saturday (24th). Last Saturday
the military, 700 strong, gave a warlike display in
this building erected by the taxpayer, and the
bagpipes subsequently made night hideous by
marching down the tram route.
** * *
On page 1 of this issue is published the
portrait of the promising Melb. soprano, Miss
Clara Mongredien. Born in Ballarat (Vic.), the
lady, when very young, was taken to Maoriland,
where, at the Dominican Convent, Dunedin, she
received her general education, as well as her first
lessons in singing. From her earliest years she
has always displayed considerable vocal ability.
Miss Mongredien returned to Melbourne five
years ago, and for three years studied singing
under Madame Fanny Simonsen. For nearly
twelve months she has been singing in public, her
first appearance being at the Metropolitan Lieder-
tafel, and very successful. For some time past
Miss Mongredien has been singing principally
at the Melbourne Exhibition concerts.

Much jealousy between the Melb. skirt-dancing schools.
One of the fashionable academies issues a notification,
hinting that the Lady Principal doesn’t like high kicking,
but if parents insist on it, the girls must go Elsewhere—
which is emphasised in a feminine way.
How the papers do rush to put in anything about an
“actrese!" Last week the gobemouches of Australia
were regaled with the doings of a “well-known actress”
over a racehorse. Certainly not one reader in 259 ever
heard of her. All this is very paltry, but it leads one to
reflect how very seldom the real members of the profession
can be laid hold of.
Pianist Bulow, whose death is cabled, was married to
Cosima, the daughter of Abbb Liszt and Countess
d’Agoult. Bulow became charmed with Wagner, and so
did Mrs. Bulow, who had the vagrant blood of her mother,
even as the blood of Maurice de Saxe coursed in the
arteries of Georges Sand. The transference of Cosima
from Bulow to Wagner was cooler than that of Lady
Millais from Ruskin.

has been purchasing plays pretty extensively of late.
Amongst others he has bought the rights of Haddon
Chambers’ new play, which is to be produced by Beerbohm-
Tree. Haddon will be much disappointed if the new play
does not make a hit. He has been commissioned by
Wyndham to write a play for him.

Another engagement in opera oomique circles.
Dick Ramsden, aged 58, died in Melb., the other day,
through a fall from a balcony, ending a miserable life.
Forty years ago, he was the most renowned boy-singer in
England. He was attached to Dean Hook’s Leeds
Church, but sang at all big gatherings, even making a
London repute. As a man he had only a thin tenor, and
formed one of Lyster’s opera chorus. Then he returned
to England, but came hack gorgeously to Australia, as a
member of the troupe which included Harry Rickards
and Lizzie Watson.

Bijou and run in opposition to Maggie Moore in “The
Golden Giant ’’ at the Royal opposite.

Grattan Riggs once staged “ Handy Andy " in a certain
town. At one point in the play, Andy, supposed to be
dead drunk, has to be carried off by Riggs, after sustaining
a heavy fall. One night, the long and lean Aubrey
Maitland, as Andy, fell heavily across a trap-door with
an iron ring in it. He had slipped his hand under him to
massage the spot, when a funny idea struck him. Passing
his hand through the waistband of his trousers, and grasp-
ing the ring firmly, he awaited developments. On came
Riggs to carry him off, but couldn’t lift him anyhow.
Riggs sought the wings, and said to one of the co.,
“ Would you take that long, lean gossoon to be so heavy ?
I couldn’t lift him! Give me some whisky to put strength
in me.” He got it, and had another try. Still he
couldn’t lift Andy. A second time Grattan sought the
wings. “Couldn’t do it, bedad. But I stirred him.
More whisky, and I’ll do it." He had a second drink,
and went on for a third try. This time Maitland had let
go the ring, so Riggs lifted him with ease. After the
show, in an adjoining bar, Riggs expatiated: “It’s wonder-
ful what strength whisky can put into a man. Look at
me to-night now 1” And he wondered why they smiled.
The chorus-singer applying for a position under Guer-
rera had no show unless his or her vocal attainments went
far beyond the pitch of the usual hack singer, professional
or amateur. Many girls, obliged by the hard times to
work for themselves, essayed to join the chorus, and
emerged from the preliminary test weeping. Unless
their knowledge exceeded the knowledge of the drawing-
room warbler, the conductor would have none of them.
Many of the old stagers who had flourished in the days of
Saurin Lyster and Simonsen broke down under the test.
That is why so many new faces were seen on the stage.
Those who “passed” to Guerrera’s satisfaction were
thorough musicians. Consequence, the chorus was by no
means the weakest part of the show, as it usually is.
The Australasian Dramatic and Musical Association, for
the succour and support of deserving old pros., would
command more respect from the public if it were worthier
of its title. Last year’s subscriptions from members
amounted to £5O. It is true that professionals bought
with their gratuitous services the proceeds’ of benefit
performances in Sydney, Melbourne, and Adelaide, but
it is equally true that many people would more gladly
patronise these annual entertainments if the association
were not helplessly dependent upon them. The claim of
the A. D. and M. Asso. upon public sympathy is slight
for the reason that it can’t pretend to be for the benefit
of poor actors and actresses in general, who, as a rule,
don’t belong to it even in the times when they might be
reasonably expected to afford a trifle. Considered as a
trade sick fund, or an anti-starvation guarantee society,
this high-sounding theatrical concern is a tinkling symbol
and a vain thing—more’s the pity.

The cables respecting the death of a lion-tamer in a
’Frisco circus go to show that light, and particularly
artificial light, is a highly necessary detail towards keep-
ing wild beasts in submission. All animals of the oat
order are bothered by intense light, and their pupils con-
tract under its influence; while man is bothered by dark-
ness. The dislike of wild beasts to fire is well-known. In
the ’Frisco tragedy, out went the electric lights ; in-
stantly the mastery of lion-tamer Thieman vanished ; the
lions sprang upon their quondam hypnotiser, and—ker-
runohl

Off the stage, strong man Sandow, short, gentle,
affable, neither suggests in appearance or manner the
man of muscle. “Here,” he said one day, “is a trick
my friend, the Emperor of Russia, who is a very powerful
man, once did in my presence.” Then Sandow took a
pack of playing-cards and tore it in half. “ Here,” he
said, “isa trick my friend, the Emperor of Russia, did
not do.” Then he placed two packs together and tore one
hundred and four cards in strips.

encore at the opera-house make up for all these priva-
tions.”
Haddon Chambers’ “The Honourable Herbert," writes
The Bulletin's London correspondent, may be cited as
a play which the public, for some occult reason, will not
take to. To my mind, it is almost, if not quite, the best
thing Chambers has done, and I feel sure that it would
be well worth trying on an Australian audience. Bad
acting killed it in London.
Dampier sometimes effectively takes to humour. A
certain tragedian had played a season in a Maori-
land city, and, on the last night, announced from
before the curtain that Dampier’g co, would open on
the following evening in the same building. Of
course, he made a patronising speech and clawed
his long hair. “ Ladies and gentlemen, while thanking
you for your kind support, &o. . . . While appearing
as »n artist before the people of New Zealand, I have
invariably been well received, and have dined with lords
and bishops. . . . Let me ask you, then, not only for
his own merits, but for my sake, to extend your warmest
patronage to my old friend, Mr. Dampier-yes, 1 may call
him my old friend—l have known and admired him for 20
years.” And so on. Dampier was in front, and heard it.
The night following the audience was somewhat meagre,
but the tragedian was there, prominent in astage-box. The
veteran Dampier came on, with a wig of gorgeous hair d
la the tragedian, only three times as long and four times
as thick, and straight off gagged a speech into the play.
“My hair, mada.ne, my hair is the admiration of the
world. Actor, madame 1 Do not call me by the plebeian
name of actor (running his hand through his tremendous
hair); lam not an actor, lam an artist! I have dined
with lords and bishops. Why, has not a member of this
very audience publicly stated that he has known and
admired me for nine hundred years—he has, by the way,
only been in the profession for ten years, yet, doubtless,
his remark is true 1” Dampier would, probably, have
gone further, but at this point the tragedian rose up and,
walked out. Then the play proceeded.

Mrs. Kendal sends tills dreadful whopper from the
United States: “ Leaving the theatre, I found that it was
raining cats and dogs. I was in a dreadful dilemma. I
had no goloshes, no umbrella. The coup 6 stood thirty
yards away, and between it and me lay a nasty big mud
puddle. 1 was just on the point of calling to Willie, when
suddenly I noticed that about twenty men in evening
clothes were waiting beside the coup 6 to catch a glimpse
of me as I passed out. As they caught sight of me stand-
ing on the threshold in perplexity, twenty crush-hats
were whisked off with one consent. There was a click like
a diminutive roll of musketry as the hats were shut, and
then, as one man, the well-dressed battalion advanced,
and laid their hats down in the mud, thus forming a series
of stepping-stones, which reached to my carriage-door.”

Last week that unspeakable financier, Treasurer
Carter, of the eternally-muddled Backbone Ministry,
announced that no more 4 per cent, debentures would be
sold in Melbourne under £lOl. A few months ago the
same blessed Govt, gave away a million's worth of bonds
to the British investor at considerably less than £96, and
just now Robert Keid is inspecting the London money-
market—probably to see if another loan can be floated at
a trifle above that figure. Apparently, the Patterson
Government is fond of the British investor—so fond of
him that it would rather take £96 from him than £lOO
from the Vic. capitalist.
Carter’s brilliant move will, of course, choke off
the local capitalist, who is hardly likely to take Vic.
debentures at £lOl when he can get those of N.S.VV. at
£lOO. Then the Backbone Cabinet will have to appeal
once more to J. Bull, and borrow another million nr two
at any price he likes to offer. There is no visible sense or
reason or sanity in Carter's proceeding ; it means loss of
self-reliance, loss of money, loss of prestige, and loss of
any little reputation for intelligence which the wretched
Backbone Cabinet ever possessed. And on the contra
side of the account there is nothing—absolutely nothing.

Last year, for the very first time since 18(59,
N.S. Wales liquidated (he interest on her public and
private debt, not out of borrowed money but out of her
own resources!!! The interest which the afflicted pro-
vince has to pay annually to the British money-lender is
estimated at £4,824,000. When the exports of N.S. Wales
exceed the imports by this amount it is evidence that
the liability has been met; when they don’t, tire interest
is being paid-either wholly or in part-out of more
borrowings from Biitain.
* * «
This is the one test which can’t be “faked.”
In the five years covered by the last Parked Foreign-trade
regime, N.S.W. did not meet one solitary penny of interest
on its public or private debt out of its onn resources—it
was all, to the last coin, paid out of borrowed money.
And the same result has characterised every Foreign-
trade boom for the past quarter of a century. In the last
34 years there have only been nine in w Inch the province
has paid any part, however small, of its interest liabilities
out of revenue, and only four in which it has liquidated
the whole bill. And these rare occasions were mug
ago, when the amount payable in interest vmis very small,
and the curse of foreign capital had not >el settled heavily
upon the land.

Moree (N.S.W.) is republican to the core.
Hassall, M.P., induced Moree’s Mayor to convene
a public meeting to consider the advisability of
inviting Gov, Duff to Moree. The Moreevians
rolled up, and it was at once moved and seconded:
“That this meeting disperse.” And it did dis-
perse there and then, and went and drank healths
to the coming Australian Republic in which
foreign Governors shall not be.

The very latest mode of reusing the wind is for
busted traders about to file bad schedules to demand
from the reconstructed hank a solatium for omitting or
reducing on their schedule the bank’s claim on the
estate. The solatium is regulated by the size of the
insolvent’s unsecured overdraft.

The elasticity of Vic. insolvency law is ap-
parently boundless. Traders threatened with insolvency
have long since jumped their private debts by forming
their businesses into limited liability co.’s (only one out
of 30 has, by the way, been upset), but a late develop-
ment shows that where one trader is heavily involved on
‘‘accommodation paper” with another, the former can,
prior to insolvency, transfer the whole of his hook-debts
(the only free asset) to his friend, and, what’s more, no
one can say “ booh ” to him. Verily is Victoria the land of
promise for the individual who is well read up in the
local insolvency laws.
* • •
Robert Smith, who carried the Mercantile Bank
prosecution successfully through the police-court, was
not tempted by the Vic. Crown Law department to follow
his case into the Criminal Court—where, as a matter of
course, he would have played only second fiddle to Walsh,
Q.O. They offered Mr. Smith a fee of £lOO for the entire
job of junior counselling, which was tantamount to
choking him off. It did choke him off. Past experiences
of the Mercantile case suggest the possibility of this final
hearing being dragged over three months.
* # •
The late committee of the South Melb. Building
Society has paid £lOOO into the bag of ruins, by way of
squaring any legal claims that Mat. Larkin’s victims may
have upon their responsibility. Leaving “dear old
Mat’s ” deficiencies out of the question, it is tolerably
certain that the committee could have been potted for the
amount of his personal guarantee (£1000) which, after the
usual custom of societies, had been allowed to lapse.
Warned by this example, a number of Melb. directorates
are taking a tender interest in the maintenance of the
sec.’s sureties,
« * #
Bruce Smith asked the Court to deal leniently
with Haviland, because “he had that loose method of
dealing with figures which was common to literary
people.” And, come to think, commercial people have
had a very loose way of dealing with figures lately.
• • •
W. H. Macnamara and S. A. Rosa get six and
three months’ gaol for libelling N.S.W. Savings Bank
trustees by selling Hard Cash. The defendants get more
sympathy than is usually accorded to law-breakers,
inasmuch as it is widely felt that the technical libel for
which they were indicted was by no means the real
cause of the prosecution.
# # •
Some Melb. brokers have been bearing Bayley’s
new shares at 15s. three months ahead. Something is
said about what a man saw who timbered a shaft, but the
writer is told that the underlay shafts are not timbered,
and as the main shaft is of course, sunk away from the
reef it is not easy to see what this rumour has to do with
the question.
« * *
Another version (on Bayley’s own authority) of
Bayley’s sale. When Sylvester Browne first asked Bayley
what he wanted for his Reward Claim, he replied: “ There
isn’t enough money in Australia to buy it.” Then, he
had no idea of selling; later on. he fell ill, and while ill
Ford, his mate, urged him to part. For some time Bayley
stuck out for £lO,OOO and one-fourth share, but finally
agreed to £6OOO and one-sixth. Bayley declares that had
he and Ford been able to leave the mine for a fly-round,
after their many hardships on the field, they would not
have sold at all.
• # •
When Bayley was in Perth offering for sale his
now famous mine, several diggers travelled there from
Coolgardie in the hope of picking up somebody with
money or influence to secure it. Everyone who looked
“ fat ” was besieged as soon as he stepped ashore at Free-
mantle by miners who told him that more gold than the
purchase money could be actually seen on Bayley’s. But
no one listened.
# * •
It is said that 14 days’ additional time was
given Browne to negotiate for Bayley’s, and that had he
liked he could have purchased Bayley’s and Ford’s 4000
original shares at 6s. each—£l4,ooo for an interest now
worth £75,000, and £6OOO for five-sixths of a mine now
worth nearly £500,000, and which, if it continues as rich
for another 100 ft., will be worth over a million.

Mount Lyell bonanzas have already returned
over 250,0000 z. silver and 70 tons copper from less than
300 tons of ore ! Reported they have a vein running up
to 12ft. thick, one-third of it worth over IOOOoz. silver to
the ton, the other part running from 30 to several hun-
dred oz. The co. now builds its own railway out ofdivs, and
sniggers at the London financiers who were too suspicious
to buy.

The lawn-tennis young man should nob rush to
Coolgardie just now. A Bulletin correspondent: “We
reached Coolgardie from Siberia after an awful time.
The bung came out of our water-cask unknown to us, so
we camped without a drop of water for horses or our-
selves, and it was over 110 in the shade, with 30 dry miles
still to go.”

tt * *
W.A. mining news is terribly mixed by the
dailies owing to the absence of communication by wire.
Under the heading of “Coolgardie goldfields” we read of
orushings and the scarcity of water at the Marble Bar. It
will be news to a lot of people to hear that Marble Bar is
a goldfield hundreds of miles north of Qeraldton, and
nearly 1000 miles from Coolgardie. Writer met a Marble
Bar miner in Melb. last week. He spoke well of the field
on which there is a 5-head battery kept going in crushing
several-oz. stone.

they have been selling admit that vendors

# • •
It is strange to know that the present N.S.W.
Agent-General discovered gold in N.S.W. six years before
Hargreaves, who got the Govt, reward for Australian
gold-discovery, and it may interest the veteran to know
that for years some hundreds of fossiokers have been em-
ployed on the banks and valleys of the Macquarie, where,
as a rule, the gold costs quite as much to get as it is
worth.

“Ergo’’has published the yields from two or
three mines at Pambula. They amount to over 13000 z.,
and he wants to know why the field is ‘ so shamefully
and persistently run down by a Sydney newspaper.
Pambula has run itself down, and two-thirds of it should
be wound-up. Two or three crushings don t make a
mine, much less a goldfield. “I have not kept the
records of other parts of this field.” “ Ergo has, there-
fore, neglected the easiest part of his duty. His state-
ment that the results of the field cannot be beaten by any
other in N.S.W. is not very wide of the mark, tor, worse
luck, there is not a payable reefing field in the province.

Some men say Murchison (where, as they have
water, they can already turn out gold from crushings) is a
greater field than waterless Coolgardie. But what Is
badly wanted is a man with Coolgardie interests who
gives his opinion in favour of the Murchison or vice versa.

It looks like touch-and-go with the New Coster-
fleld CO., formed to treat the accumulated tailings. Cost
of extraction greater than anticipated, and treatment
more difficult where the tailings are less oxidised.
“ Bob ” Beeston holds the big end, and if he pulls the
concern through will deserve more than he is likely to
make out of it.
