The inventor of a gold-saving machine seeks to
claim the £lOOO reward offered by N.S.W. Mines Depart-
ment for the discovery of a new payable goldfield, but
Govt, says he doesn’t come within the category of eh-
gibles. Well, it all depends on the machine. A real,
upstanding, whole-souled saver would actually discover 50
new payable goldfields by galvanising the ghosts of the
old ones.

Some ignorant new - chums out prospecting
dropped across some rich specimens, and, on returning
to camp, showed them and “ blew ” about them, explain-
ing exactly where the pieces had come from. Two or
three sharp people “ took it all in,” and one of these,
feigning illness, disappeared from view-for the purpose
of pegging-out the new-chums’ ground. Later on, the
smartiea accompanied the new-chums to the latter’s
ground, and were, of course, much surprised and rather
indignant to discover that the new arrivals “had been
working on our prospecting area. Why, there’s our pegs.
Don’t you see ’em? Hey!” That is the story told of
how one of the richest Northern mines tell into the
hands of its late owners.
* • •
Australian Broken Hill Consols has happened
on another bonanza —the largest since that of 1891. The
find comprises native silver and fahlerz, the former
occurring in slugs of several pounds weight. Opportune,
as the patience of the shareholders, mostly English, was
almost exhausted.
* # •
Junction North (8.H.), having lost the run of
its garnet sandstone body, has been anxiously looking for
same for some time past. Result, no garnet sandstone,
but much sulphide ; value of latter at present, nil.
# • *
Block 10 prospects steadily on the down grade.
The western lode at 1015 ft. is a dense sulphide high in
zinc and low in silver and lead—the sort of material cal-
culated to produce chronic nightmare in a seeker after
the solution of the sulphide problem. The lode was found
in a drive east from Campbell’s shaft.
* • *

» * *
Colossal sums have been spent in Barrier sul-
phide experiments. First the Proprietary Co. imported
Dr Schnaebel, from Freiburg, at a huge fee, and since
then practically every B.H. mine manager has been
experimenting—at shareholders’ expense. The number
of discarded experimental plants is legion. Shoals ot
private inventors have also been at work. Though special-
class sulphides are made to yield a profit, the real
“ solution of the sulphide problem ” is not yet.
Most of the processes have been either mechani-
cal or chemical. Two Gilbertian students, however, tried
something humorous boiling oil and melted lead.
Patents not yet out.
# # *
Marshall Brothers’ Hill End reef is a continuation
of Sftrgeant’s vein, west of and within the township area.
Nearly 40 years ago, when working the matrix for gold
was prohibited, thousands of ounces were obtained 200
yards south of the present find. Fully 500 ft. along the
vein has been exposed, showing splendid specimens. Over
£BOO in value has been taken out, and the picked quartz
would realise over lOOoz. to the ton, while the average
should be over 30oz. to the ton. Professors Taiband
Watson, of Adelaide University, were on the ground
when the find was reported, and themselves knocked out
£3OO worth of specimens in a couple of hours. The result
is the formation of a wealthy S.A. syndicate, headed by
Barr Smith, to work the reef. The specimens have been
compared with Bayley’s and are said to look even better.
The wages difficulty of the Newcastle and Bulli
coal-miners ami of Custom House tide-waiters is, in the
public eye, confused because high rates are paid for hourly
or daily work without the number of working hours or
days in the week or year being specified. The coal-miner
makes from 15s. to 20s. per day, but take the year, and he
makes about £6O; the tide-waiter makes 10s. 6d. per day
and 15s. per night, but his annual income, in N.S.W., i»
about £BO.

After another brief spurt, Drake, N.S.W.,
appears to be going back to the melancholy churchyard
solemnity which characterised it some years ago when its
batteries were deserted and its overflowing mining dams
covered with wild duck.
* • *
“Wanted, two plasterers, contract big country
job, labour only.” In reply to which Sydney Morning
Herald advt.,' 419 plasterers turned up at the given
address. The chosen ones could get railway carriage
part of the way, but would have to refund first chance,
and pay their own coach-tares from railway to destina-
tion. All were eager for the job, and 417 envious un-
employed plasterers filed out of the temporarily-blocked
offices.

# * *
They talk of exploring the deep level of Maori-
land goldfields with the diamond drill. The Big Pump
shaft at the Thames should be a good point of commence-
ment. Over £3,000,000 of gold have been won from reefs
within a quarter of a mile, but the shots petered out
below 400 ft. Twenty years ago H. A. Severn, a clever
metallurgist and scientist, predicted that these shots
would probably make again at 1500 ft.
» • •
The Argus is becoming fiercely Democratic.
“ The salaries and wages of those engaged on the trams
have been brought down to the lowest level. No one can
desire that further reductions should be made in the
earnings of those who are responsible for the working of
the system.” Having got the horse down to one straw a
day, no one desires to split that straw. The Argus is
prepared to take a noble and decisive stand in the interests
of the workers, when the co. calls upon them to live on
saw'dust and exhausted machine-oil.
* # •
A light on the Matabele question : Knox, mem-
ber for Cavan, recently suggested that the Chartered Co.
had placed their shares judiciously, and that was why the
Commons had not protested against the Matabele war.
Knox further stated in the House that, to his personal
knowledge, several members had been allotted shares,
payable 3s. on allotment, which they could sell in the city
next day for £l. And he refused to withdraw his state-
ment.
• • •
Re the scheme to provide for the unemployed
by establishing big fisheries in the islands of Bass's
Straits, it has been reported to the Tas. autho i ies that
fish are scarce, the land unproductive, and that the whole
group of islands so swarms with snakes that settlement
would be most dangerous. This reminds the writer of a
fishing and pigeon-shooting party to Dent Island, Whit-
sunday Passage, which shot 30 snakes in two hours, and
then had to clear out of their camp and take to the boat Io
save their lives.

“Look here, Abraham, I can’t possibly meet
that bill due to-morrow. Hold It over for a day or two,
will you?” “ Mein goot frendt, I vould be delighted to
do it, put the fact is, I’m in titficulties meinselluf, and
had to tisgount your pill mit a frendt, put I’ll tell you vat
I’ll do (as you are an oldt gustomer of mine): I’ll see if I
can’t arranche it for you at a brice.” “What price,
Abraham ?” “Veil, say a gommission of viftey per cent,
on renewal.” “ Say 25 per cent., and do your best.”
Arrangement made, and borrower pays 50 per cent, for
renewal to third party and 25 per cent, to the original
lender as commission. It may be hard to believe, but
this is what one of the ’cutest men in Melbourne did last
week without tumbling to the solemn fact Abraham
(the original lender) still held his (the borrower’s) bill,
and simply set up the mythical third party as holder of
the document to get an extra 25 per cent.

The best London circular on Australian mines
is probably that issued by S. James, a member of the

Australian goldmines in preference to those of either
South Africa or America.
• • •
Even the milkman exudes a moral these topsy-
turvy days. In and around Sydney he sells his produce
to cheese-factories at 3d. per gallon, and to private cus-
tomers at Is. 4d., and rejoices in the first instance, where-
as in the other he desponds. Why ? Because 3d. in the
hand seems better to him than 16d. in the Small Debts
Court.

# * •
The great drawback to the sale of Australian
frozen meat in London is (hat, when thawed, it becomes
sloppy and flabby and as uninviting in appearance as the
body of a badly worried-by-dogs bandicoot. The Ameri-
can beef, brought to London, chilled only, has a bright,
inviting colour and brings 2d. per lb. more than the
frozen Australian meat.

Editor Bulletin,—Re your par. about W. H.
Beers’ policy in the Mutual Life of N.Y. The total
premiums paid amounted to nearly double the face-value
of the policy, while the amount paid as a loss only repre-
sents about 2 per cent, compound interest on premiums
paid, and this is after 43 years. Not so wonderful when
worked out.—lnsurance.

on the list isn’t surnamed Stephen.
Dear Bulletin, —In Tor-
onto, Canada, recently, I met
contractor Goddard, uncle to the
Barrier champion, a sturdy little
man with many old-time Syd-
n y reminiscences. Architect
Barnett was originally a stone-
mason in Goddard’s em-
ploy, and got his first
lift when Goddard re-
commended him as a
timekeeper to B aokett,
then building Sydney
University. Over a glass
of ala we discussed the political
genius of Parkes. Goddard re-
membered his first election for
East Sydney, when he was
opposed by Kemp, of the
Herald, a jolly, little, round
man. When Kemp appeared at
the hustings to address the
electors his pants were badly
burst behind. Parkes followed
him, and commenced thus: “You
have heard a speech from my
opponent, in which he has re-
minded you of a great many
fine things. The only thing he
reminds me of is a defective
orange, and the more I look at
him the more defective he ap-
pears.” Hasn’t that the real
Parkesian flavour?—Yours, Aus-
tralian.

and may serve him.
Sir Arthur Stepney, bart.
and millionaire, is unobtrusively
touring Australia, picking up
property bargains at depresh.
prices. He has just invested a
few shekels in Westralia. Sir
Arthur has a courteous manner
and wears shocking clothes, as
befits a man of lucre. His
favourite amusement is whis-
tling, and his sole exercise is
to twiddle two coppers and a
florin together. This is a
“sodety” item without 1 the
jargon, yet it properly fills the
bill about Baronet Stepney.

Archdeacon Green, of Ballarat, who has
been chosen Bishop of Grafton and Armidale
(N.S.W.), is the first graduate of an Australian
University raised to the Episcopal bench. He is
only 37 years of age and an excellent speaker.

A Darlinghurst gaoler says that prisoners
addicted to the composition of poetry are the
most tractable of culprits. “If they are let go
up and down their cells and scribble a bit from
time to time, the devil a bit of trouble they give.”
The eyeglass of dear old G. H. Reid,
M.P., the good-natured leader of N.S.W. Opposi-
tion, is not a mere ornament, as so many people
suppose. He wears it to prop his eye open when
he’s talking. If he didn’t he’d talk himself t >
sleep.

A London publisher, writing to a Sydney

Gladstonian decay progresses slowly. The
old man is now going blind from cataract, and
urges an operation, which the doctors refuse. In
the course of the next few weeks it will be dis-
covered that he is also lame, deaf, and paralytic.
This gradual accumulation of diseases is meant to
prepare the public mind for resignation, and to
let the Liberals down easily. Already the Tories
are preparing their Cabinet.
The late E. Milner Stephen, N.S.W.
Official Assignee, who died last week, was born in
Van Diemen’s Land in the reign of Gov. Arthur,
ihere were 10 Stephens at the funeral, but
deceased’s father, the patriarchal head of the
family, was absent.
As the world knows, Kinnaird Rose,
barrister and journalist, had his magnificent red
beard mutilated by a malignant enemy as he slept
the sleep of exhaustion on a lounge at Q. Parlia-
ment House, Brisbane, while awaiting the con-
clusion of a debate. It is not generally known.
Photo, by Talma] [Swanston-st., Melb.
Miss CLARA MONGREDIEN.
however, that when some well-meaning friend
offered to tell Rose the name of the perpetrator,
lie refused to hear it. “By God,” he said, “I
would not know his name, for if I did I believe I
would have to kill him.”
The S. A. police recently laid an informa-
tion against Bartlett, M.L.A , who was charged
with criminally assaulting a girl of seven years.
The charge was ferreted out by a man who not
long ago unsuccessfully sued the accused for
canvassing money. Bartlett was honourably
acquitted.
Archdeacon Bentzen, of Mudgee, who
died lately, was a Dane by birth, and though he
knew nothing about the English language till he
came, a full-grown man, to Australia, he for many
years past enjoyed the reputation of being one of
the most accurate and graceful speakers of English
in the Australian church.

Panton, P.M., Melb., is still the foe of
would-be suicides. Said lie last Thursday : “We
cannot allow these fellows who look upon death so
lightly to escape some punishment.” The suicides
want to appear before a higher court, but in
Panton’s mind the majesty of Heaven is nothing
to the awe-provoking awfulness of a Smellbourne
beak.

Dibbs was only able to poll one vote for
statesmanship at a recent meeting of Sydney
Unitarian Society, while Parkes scored thirteen
(ominous number!). Dibbs had therefore better
make a virtue of necessity and clear. There is
a great opening for him just now at Coolgardie,
where they are crying out for a big public dam.
Dibbs can do it, every time.
The heart of Samuel Wilson was made
glad last week by a letter from Lome (Vic.)
announcing the capture of a 281 b. salmon in the
Erskine River hard by. It was thereabouts that
the knight of the auburn whiskers had sown some
infant salmon years ago, dreaming of a glorious
future when the name of Wilson will be preserved
on ice in every fislmfbngers’ shop, so to speak.
Posterity promises to rank him higher than his
fellow squatters who laid the foundations of
rabbits and snails in their adopted country.
The statement that “no man named Joseph
ever comes to much ” is resented as a personal
insult by Joseph Greer, of Sydney Labour Bureau.
Joseph lately asked : “ Who was the first historical
Joseph? Did he not rise in Egypt from the
humblest rank to bo a greater power in the king-
dom than Bismarck has been in Germany? And
was Josephus nobody? And what about Gari-
baldi and Mazzini, a pair of Josephs? And where
is Addison, the essayist, and Scaliger, the critic,
and De Maistre, the controversialist? And what
about Hume, the historian and political reformer ?
And is Joe Oliamberlain nobody? Considering
that the name Joseph is given to children only
once for every dozen and score of times John and
James and William are given, the illustrious
Josephs are numerically in the ascendant.” Then
Mr. Greer took a drink of water.

no starch about you.”

(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Out from the kraal with the carcase ! Fling out
the carrion, fling !
Let the Matabele lions rend the Matabele king !
Yet lions would scorn such offal—this but a”dog
hath been !
Call hither the vile hyena, summon the bird
obscene;
Let the unclean vultures gorge their fill of un-
clean flesh and gore,
For the Lord thy God is a jealous God, the Lord!
is a man of war.
My children coveted gold that lay in the savage,
land ;
They stretched forth their hand and took, for L
had strengthened their hand.
Then this dead thing—it is this ! that dared t'o>
defy my might—
This prayed to its fetish dim, and flouted the God!
of Light.
The savages fought and fought until they couldi
fight no more,
For the Lord thy God is a terrible God, the Lordl
is a man of war.
Charity ? Justice ? Mercy ? Myths of a grey
old creed !
A holier gospel is preached bv Cant, the beautiful
gospel Greed.
Ihe savages fought, as savages will, for wife and
babe and hearth ;
T hey bared their breasts and shook their spears,
and charged and bit the earth ;
My children scattered them as the wind the chaff
on the threshing-floor,
For the Lord thy God is the Lard of Hosts, the
Lord is a man of war.
Let the_ altar smoke and the censer swing in the
shrine of the great and good,
But the savour of incense is not so sweet as the
savour of fresh-spilt blood ;
Let the chant be raised by white-robed priests in
the dim cathedral fane,
But the voice of the choir is not so sweet as the
shriek of the dying man ;
There is joy in the pangs of the parting soul whem
the slaughter has been full sore,
ror the Lord is the God of battles, the Lord is ai
man of war.
So, out from the kraal with the carcase! Fling,
out the carrion, fling !
Let the Matabele lions rend the Matabele king !'
Yet lions would scorn such offal—this but a dog
hath been 1
Call hither the vile hyena, summon the bird:
obscene;
Let the unclean vultures gorge their fill of uncleani
flesh and gore,
For the Lord thy God is a vengeful God, the Lordl
is a man of war.
A. G. STEPHENS.

Per head. This doesn’t mean more teetotallers—

N.S.W. Commandant Hutton’s 14-times-a-
-week-repeated argument that the more money
Australia spends on military defence, the more it
will be able to borrow in England, comes simply to
this, that the more Australia borrows from Eng-
land the more she must spend on militarism.
Jabez Balfour, let it be noted, is not
charged with issuing false balance-sheets, but with
“ fraud as director of a public company, and
obtaining money by false pretences.” Yet the
Jabez Balfour companies were worked upon the
same lines as many Australian land-boom institu-
tions, doing a flourishing paper business among
themselves, creating false profits, and disbursing
large dividends which really came from the capital
and deposits. If Jabez be extradited and put
upon his trial in London, a full account of the
proceedings will doubtless interest the Victorian
Crown Law department.

It would appear that one
of the future troubles of “New
Australia” is to arise out of
the fact that the land is al-
ready largely occupied by Indian
settlers. These settlers, appa-
rently, have no legal title to
the soil; still, they have built
theirhomes there and established
their farms, and the New Aus-
tralians will have to evict them
in the good old Irish fashion
before they can enter into full
possession. In fact, the commu-
nity, where all is to be peace
and plenty and universal brother-
hood, can’t get fairly started
until these unfortunates are
bounced out to take their chance,
with their wives and families,
in the hungry outer world. Par-
aguay, by the way, is reported
to be a place where the public-
houses are open all Sunday, and
where the day of rest is mostly
put in at cock-fighting in the
public-house yard. These cir-
cnmstances don’t promise well for a strictly-
teetotal settlement.

which, of late, have been competing with N.S.W.
railways on the journey to Sydney, are not all run
on their own wheels, as it were, but that subsidies
are paid to at least some of them by interested
persons who wish to discredit the N.S.W. railway
management.

Dear Bulletin, —Who is it that is entitled
to the undying contempt of white men for first in-
troducing Kanakas to Queensland ? I know that
Gilchrist Watt had them on May Downs, 50
miles from St. Lawrence, in 75, when I was there
jaokarooing. Wellshot is said to have been the
first Kanaka home on the Barcoo. Who was the
first “ blackbirder ” ?—Yours, Jeeby,
Lobengula, the deceased Matabele king,
to J. Ball’s Chartered 00. : —“ Land is where my
women plant their corn; land is where my people’s
cattle feed; land is where my young men hunt;
land is the sole livelihood and birthright of my
people. I have no power to cede my people’s land
away to white men.”
The Adelaide unemployed supply an ex-
cellent illustration of the folly which is eagerly
seized upon by opponents to damn the cause of
Labour. They want a private subscription started;
they want church collections ; they want Govt, to
issue free rations. But they won’t crack stones in
return :jfche work is degrading.

The Yic. Public Works Department is
now doing its best to encourage convict-labour on
State contracts, having induced the penal authori-
ties to undercut the prices of legitimate firms who
tender for supplying broken metal— 2Jin. bluestone
broken for Is. 4d. a yard ! The practice of buying
clothing, boots, etc., from Pentridge prison is also
being encouraged amongst Government employes.

in all its shapes and forms, freedom of exchange
is gained the association will have done its work,”
says the ingenuous secretary. At which rate the
Crack of Doom will find that syndicate of patriots
still working.

LEARNING HIS PROFESSION.
PARSON: “It’s shocking to hear a boy like you, swearing to
himself for mere fun, as you think it.”
BOY (who is temporarily imagining log to he a sulking bullock) :
“I’m not sweatin’ to myself for fun, I’m learning to
drive bullocks. I’m going to drive father’s bullocks
next shearin’.”
Convention, is pretty well quenched. What’s the
use of illusions ? All the sludge of Trenwith, of
Hancock, of Murphy ?
No matter what may be thought of its
propriety, the pluckiest political action for many
years was the Dibbs Govt’s, retention of Minister
Copeland, in defiance of Mr. and Mrs. Grundy.
Nothing like it has been seen since the time of
Milburn Creek, when gallant old Jack Robertson
stuck to Garrett, while Parkes threw Baker to the
wolves. Not that The Bulletin thinks Baker
deserved anything better.
Why don’t the unemployed women of
Sydney interview the Premier by deputation?
The appearance and re-appearance of unemployed
men only in the capacity of seekers for work is a
mere perpetuation of the old faith that women,
when they suffer, must suffer in silence. Women’s
hearts are being broken daily for want of work,
and they have as much right as the men to be-
labour Government for help.
Dear Bulletin, —You remark on the
absence of “great little men” from Australian
politics. There are plenty of great big men “in
it,” but why? Because broad shoulders and bull-
necks, and York-ham fists, and the bellowing
power of a bison, are the essentials to success in
Australian politics just now. When statesman-
ship and patriotism are wanted again the little
man may show up. lam a small man.—Yours?
Smell.
The S.M. Herald stints its admiration of
the zealots who maintain that only “ Christians ”
should be elected to Parliament. It naively
argues that, were our coinage pure gold, the metal
would melt away in course of circulation, and
that in a similar way Christian men without the
alloy of a few vices would be too soft for legisla-
tive work. People will now clearly understand
why it is that “ Mister James ” has so repeatedly
declined the invitations of Freetrade leagues to
stand for Parliament.
The Earl of Radnor, a recent peer of no
special account, declares that the House of Lords
won’t be abolished except by a gory revolution.
The peers, in fact, are going to wade in blood and
carnage before they let go one of the blessed
privileges of the House which rules because it is
somebody’s eldest son. All the same, when the
time comes, they will knock under unobtrusively.
There is more beer than blood and old tradition in
the Lords nowadays, and beer seldom fights.
Miller, M.P., said the other night that if
there were no N.S.W. Hansard, Parkes and
Dibbs would deny that they made such speeches
as afterwards turned out to be inconvenient to
them. Quite likely; but when Mr. Miller nodded,
“ And so would I,” he suggested the preacher who
quoted St. Paul and nodded, “ My opinion is just
the same.” It is certainly worth while to keep a
Hansard to choke Parkes or Dibbs, but it would
be ruinous waste to keep a five-page primer to
choke a battalion of Millers.
A N.S.W. country bailiff has been made
returning-officer for his electorate. This is a fine
stroke of policy. Hitherto it has been possible to
dodge a local writ; but now in our mind’s eye,
Horatio, do we see the bailiff sitting in his booth
and serving out blue-paper and ballot-papers at
one and the same time. His minions will all be
poll-clerks and assistants, and will catch the un-
wary voter on the hop. Why shouldn’t all the
returning-officers be bailiffs, or all the bailiffs be
returning-officers, and kill two birds with one
stone in the interests of the groaning taxpayer?
Premier Patterson (he has been staying at
Richmond, N.S.W., with Brokenhillionaire Philip
Charley, whose romanticised history was lately
set forth in the daily press) insists blatantly that
the fears about the horrors of winter to the unem-
ployed are groundless. “As a matter of fact,” he
says, “ there is no winter in this country.” It is
a great pity that Patterson cannot be given six
months on the doss without the option of a fine.
Considering the heat of the Melb. summer, a
Melb. winter is a cruel ordeal to the unsheltered.
A new paper, called the Orangeman, is
shortly to be started in Sydney. Its mission is to
boom the memory of Dutch William and the
siege of ’Derry, and incidentally to give a helping
hand to Freetrade, on the good old assumption
that God is on the side of imported calico. The
Cardinal, by the way, is a Freetrader, but as the
Neild crowd doesn’t allow any Catholic to be a
Freetrader he will probably be blasted with the
rest of the Protectionist party. The Orangeman
is to be run by a Hebrew gentleman, on the
“How these Christians love one another” prin-
ciple.
The old English law forbidding the settle-
ment of Jesuits within British territory has never
been repealed in N.S.W. Therefore, if J. Cash
Neild, M.P., likes to rise like one strong Orange-
man in his fury, and take the requisite legal
proceedings, he can hunt away every Jesuit, and
run the emissaries of Rome into the South Sea.
His neglect of this great work makes The Bul-
letin half inclined to suspect that Jawbone is
not a true Orangeman, after all. In fact, this
paper doesn’t feel at all certain that he isn’t a
Popish emissary in disguise.
A member of Melb. Trades Hall council
lately drew attention to the alarming local
increase of Chinese laundries (there are no
Chinese laundries in Sydney—why ?) and said
that the working class were their best customers.
This is one of the awkward facts which persist in
cropping up to show that the working man is no
more of an angel than anybody else. It is generally
the’wife who is to blame, however. “ The practi-
cal animal ” will always prefer a Chinese to a
European cabbage if she can get it for a farthing
less, and often when the price is the same, “John
is so civil!” As a preparation for female suffrage,
working men might try to educate their wives and
daughters in the knowledge that these small
economies won’t work with the great principles
which mean, not economy only, but existence for
them all.

PREMIER PATTERSON—UNDER ALTERED CONDITIONS.
"There is no use in fifty premiers preachiug to the helpless unemployed multitude the doctrine of ‘self reliance,’ and reminding them of the wonders which a
man could perform 30 years ago, with only sixpence in his pocket, when he earned his swag and billy, and broiled his steak at a roadside fire on the point of a stick.
All the conditions of those times have passed away. The soil, which is the matrix of industry, was not monopolised. Labour was then everywhere in request,
Now it is a drug in the market. If Mr. Patterson were to stand to-day on the city pavement, an unknown stranger, with only sixpence in his pocket and self-reliance
in his bosom, he would run a great risk of having to walk the streets for want of a bed, and with the certainty of looking to the street tap for his breakfast and morn-
ing ablutions."-MELB. AGE.

Sydney Evening News states that Queen
Victoria wears white stockings and elastic-side
boots. There is presumably no legal remedy for
this outrage, but if Victoria were to start out one
of her Hindoo servants to kill Henniker Heaton,
who is the Evening News’ near relative, the
great soul of the country would be with her. The
newspaper proprietary which accuses any lady of
wearing white stockings and elastic boots should
be outlawed.
# # *
Dear Bulletin, —A contributor praises
the Austral Salon for being quite able to manage
its money affairs, and in spite of the prevailing
depression, having ,£257 to the good. The ex-
planation is simple. Beyond the rent, it had
hardly any other calls on its bank account, for,
whenever the Salon wanted anything, some mem-
ber of the committee would start a fund, and go
round with the hat, into which the women would
drop church-silver, but every unfortunate male
associate was asked to contribute gold. At least,
that was my experience several times. This also
explains why there was, in the early days of the
Salon, such a diligent canvass after male-associates.
When the Austral Salon is on the warpath, Ah
Sin is not “in it.’’—Yours, &c., Ex.-Male-
Associate.
* • *
The odd circumstance connected with the
irregularities of the latest levanting Vic. Civil
servant, is that he was not “greatly respected by
all who knew him.” He comes under the news-
paper description of “highly popular,” which
means that he rampaged at hotel-bars, and made
himself favourably known to reporters by his
eternal thirst. An appalling statement that he
was “the very last man who would have been
suspected of any irregularity,” will set a lot of
people wondering where they ought to look for the
first man entitled to such suspicion.
# • *
A particularly catchy advertisement—in a
leading Australian daily—for a governess attracted
over 200 applications, from “ society girls ’’ mostly,
who are now only too anxious to turn their hands
to anything. The lady, chosen by personal inter-
view—a young and pretty girl—has ordered, by
direction, several new frocks, and is to proceed,
via a popular health resort, to the scene of her
labours accompanied by her employer, whose wife
and family happen just now to be away in
England. #
A U.S. army officer is liable to be “re-
tired ” for obesity. What a spectacle a Queen’s
Birthday review in Victoria must be to a Yankee
military man!

If there is one point
on which Maoriland law
is more strict than an-
other it is that the wit-
ness at an inquest must
* 1 see the body. ” The sta-
tute; of the province are
built to fall like a billion
bricks on the witness who
doesn’t make a sufficient
inspection of the “body.”
At a recent Auckland
inquest, held upon some
disfigured and almost un-
recognisable remains, the
wiiow of the deceased
was taken to view the
body according to law.
She fainted at the door
of the morgue, and a com-
passionate constable com-
promised with the law by
carrying her in and out
again. Then the defunct
was removed for burial.
But at the inquest it
transpired that the widow
hadn’t “seen the body”
as provided by law, so a
constable ran after the
cadaver and brought it
back, and the coffin was
unscrewed, and the wit-
ness, in a partially in-
sensible state, was as-
sisted out and “saw the
body.” Then the con-
stable announced that the
body had been duly seen,
and the law was satisfied.
Antoinette Sterling was lionised one day
by tbe Austral Salon, Melbourne. Asked to speak,
she mounted the platform, and requested the one
man present (he was quite tame and had been
handing around tea) to withdraw. This request
he ignored by backing against the wall and staring
stonily into space. “Well, I warn you,” said
Antoinette, “that I’m going to say some very
unpleasant things.” Then she launched out for
two hours against all the vices of humanity, finish-
ing up by a burst of universal brotherhood. “I
love everybody,” she exclaimed; “I love all
women, I love all men—yes, I even love all
reporters.” At the close of her oration she swept
her way to the door, kissing to right and left;
in front of the solitary male she paused, and,
murmuring “Is he not a man and a brother?’’
seized the astonished and now blushing youth
round the neck, firmly pressing her lips upon his
manly brow. Then that youth darted downstairs
in search of the nearest bar.
# * *
If the W.C.T.U. saints were better up in
classical history they would not probably have
chosen saffron-coloured window-curtains for their
new premises in Flinders-st., Melb. It may lead
to awkward mistakes in that locality. The tout
ensemble can only be perfected by green shutters
and occasional glimpses of pink-robed ladies
with straw-coloured hair.
By the way, the W.C.T.U. ladies have
celebrated the opening of their new abode by
attacking the enemy in the form of the ice-cream
man. This vessel of wrath lies in wait for Sunday-
school children on their way to school, and
intercepts the missionary-box pennies. Horrified
at seeing the collection and the ice-cream melt
away simultaneously, the ladies interviewed Col-
lingwood City Council, which hit back and talked
quite straight about the religion of a society
that strains at the gnat in Sunday penny ices,
and swallows the camel in encouraging the free
discussion of the most revolting subjects by women
before mixed audiences, including young children.
* » *
The son of a Northern squatter, who has
been South for holidays, offered his hand and
fortune to a Sydney girl, lately, and was condi-
tionally accepted. The condition embraced a
signed and attested statement of his actual
interest in his father’s property, the pragmatic
girl asserting that she “ wanted to keep out of
anything like Carcoar business.”
* » *
The figment under which Australian
Governors are Imperial commanders-in-chief,
enables their military aides-de-camp to set down
the social services of their term at Government
House as services rendered in the army. It was
on this footing that Rupert Leigh, Lady Jersey s
brother and Gov. Duff’s aide, obtained promotion
to a majorship, the other day.
* * *
Editor Bulletin, — The development of
canine rabies in the hitherto exempt Gape Colony
has an unbounding interest for this cur-cursed
community. Of Sydney’s 20,000 dogs or so, not
half contribute to the revenue, and not one-
twentieth are of any use except circulate
hydatids and render the brain-worker’s night a
hell. In my street, of 41 houses, there are 33
dogs—all curs, all vociferous, and not a few
vicious. On account of the hydatid horror alone
the dog-tax should long ago have been increased
ten-fold.—Growl.

An inhuman story is told of the wife of a
British M.P. who lately flew into a rage when her
husband informed her that he was to be put down
for a baronetcy on the New Year’s list of baubles,
The lady’s indignation was such that the culprit
tottered off to Gladstone, or the person who super-
intends these little matters, and begged to have
his claim to distinction scratched off the paper.
Tothe ordinary woman of Australian “society” this
yarn must sound incredible. The craving for ft
title is born in her, like a secret passion for
diamonds and a thinly-disguised admiration for
curly-haired young men. In the absence of further
particulars as to this mysterious rejection of a
baronetcy out of deference for the wife’s feelings
one can only suppose that the wife was a courtesy
“Lady” already—the daughter of a peer. In
that case, of course, she would prefer the idea of
“Mr. and Lady Clarissa Jones” to the idea of
“ Sir John Jones, Bart., and Lady Jones.” The
plain mister, who marries an earl’s daughter, must

The Chicago contingent from Sydney, says
The Bulletin’s London correspondent, are fast
arriving in London, The little Doctor is here,
and Bruce, Terry, and Lew Sampson are on
hand. Several of the staff are going to ’Frisco,
where there is some kind of exhibition on. Terry
has some design in connection with the Antwerp
show. It is strange how men, once they become
connected with exhibitions, can never quite turn
to anything else. It is like journalism, gold-
mining, or the African exploration fever. Once
bitten by it, you can never settle down again to
steady work. # #
Editor Bulletin, —When women’s suffrage
is universal in Australia, probably the unionist’s
wife will be placed on the same footing as himself.
She will refuse to work 16 hours a day for seven
days a week, with little or no pay for her labour,
save a grumble when she fails to keep the children
quiet, or when the meal is a few minutes late.—
Saba.
* • *
The next council-clerk of Burwood, Syd-
ney —the last one, who was also engineer and over-
seer of works, after fatally shooting the Mayor,
Vdew out his brains last week, should be a
cast-iron man, like “Banjo’s” “Canvasser.’
Aldermen are a quarrelsome lot, but for hot-headed
bickering Burwood heads the list. The meetings
pass off quietly enough, but it has not been un-
common for one mad alderman to be seen hunting
round for another alderman’s gore. No wonder
that Redfearn went looking for blood as well;
nobody expected more from his threats than
another exhilarating scuffle at the outside.
Is it a fact, as stated, that distressed Burwood
residents have had their little “ sticks ” sold up re
trivial sums owing for rates, while aldermen were
allowed to remain unsued for large amounts ?
Privileges of this kind appear to be too often
aldermanic perquisites.
A recent European scandal has enabled the
Tory press to define and grade a few society terms.
When an amorous princess elopes with a mashing
officer, doesn’t get married, but lives in free love
and retirement with him, it is, if you please, a
“ romantic affair.” Indeed, some papers style it
a “romantic marriage .” When two common
persons, male and female, “jump over the broom-
stick,” it is a vile illustration of the vicious
instincts of the lower orders.—
A prince’s lenian, they still adore ;
A poor man’s mistress, they call a .
* # *
The missionaries of the Batavia River
have scored this time. Had the Kanahooka sur-
vivors landed there some few years ago, they
would probably have been made cold meat of very
quickly. Altogether, they may consider they
have had a marvellous escape. Had they drifted
into the centre of the Gulf, their chance of being
picked up would have been small indeed, for there
it is a sea without a sail. The mail-steamers cross
the mouth from point to point. The Norrnanton
boats keep up to the western side, and the occa-
sional boat to the Roper and Maoarthur keeps to
the east. There is nothing to take a craft in to
the centre of the Gulf. It is, perhaps, for its
locality, the most lonely patch of sea in the world.
* * *
An up-country functionary who, with two
companions, visited Sydney within the last two
years to interview Minister Lyne regarding local
requirements, got suddenly lost from his hotel.
Three days afterwards he reappeared, and alleged
that he had passed the interval as a guest of the
Minister. His brother functionaries have, how-
ever, since worked up dates and incidents which
make it appear that their chief, under art assumed
name, had “done time’* in Sydney gaol
inebriety in the interval, and they challenge
Minister LynC to refute the charge. Lyne.iS a
wily man who, he remarked lately, neither
admits nor refutes anything about anybody any*
where except in Parliament) and only o sionally
then.

Yanraon are doing wonderfully well in
Melbourne of late. They get double rates for
“ shifting” by night, and everybody in Mell u <e
shifts by night now.
Within the last
three years about 100
murdered infants have
been found in Mel-
bourne, and those
which have not been
found may amount to
another hundred, or,
it may be, to another
thousand. Among
all the multifarious
assassins Francis
Knorr was the only
one discovered. Her
death is a solemn
evidence that in-
fanticide is a safe
pursuit if the infant
is only buried deep
enough, but that
murder will out when
the baby-farmerleaves
the dead offspring in
her own backyard,
with only six inches
of soil above its re-
mains. Therefore her
voice cries out from
the grave to bid the
baby - farmer go and
inter her dead behind
one of those lone-
some, mouldering
villas at Elsternwick
which Murphy built
for the Premier Per-
manent Building
Society, and there it
will be unseen and
untroubled until the
liquidation gives up
its dead.

A Yic. heiress
lately married a * ‘ two-

The British clergy, with typical fatuity,
protest against the habit of coroners’ juries bring-
ing in verdicts of “suicide whilst of unsound
mind,” as calculated to remove the stigma that
attaches to the act of self-slaughter :
Avaunt 1 we cannot hope to tell
How we despise such canting bluff.
The “ suicide ” has gone to hell—
Say, parson, isn’t that enough ?
At a morgue inquest at South Sydney,
on a Chinaman’s wife who had hanged herself,
the constable who cut her down was observed
affectionately nursing something in a white hand-
kerchief. When he came to give his evidence, he
carefully unfolded the parcel and laid the contents
before the coroner. It was the cord wherewith
the deed had been done.
* * *
It is rumoured that a Southern Judge, who,
stands 4ft. lOin. in his boots, is contemplating
matrimony for the third time. It is related that, in
answer to a bumptious lawyer, who once threatened
to put him (the Judge) in his pocket, his Honor
replied: “Then you’ll have more brains there
than you carry in your hat.’’
* * *
A police charge : “ Stealing 100 sheets of
bark, the property of Her Majesty.” And this,
up at Dungog (N.S.W.) Have Australians any
sense of shame? Nay, have they any sense of
humour? Why not: “Extracting a stump, the
property of Her Majesty?” As if it were the
old lady’s very last tooth.

Dear Bulletin, The story told by
“Sappho Smith ” in last week’s issue, concerning

A SUMMER NOCTURNE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Night gave to thee thy shadowy hair,
The star-shine brightens in thine eyes,
The languid sweetness of the dusk
Pervades thy low replies.
The mystery of summer night
Is in thy lightest action shown,
Hinting of sweet and subtile things
Known but to thee alone.
Thine arms are as a wished-for rest
Coming when day is done,
To lie at peace upon thy breast
Is shadow after sun.
2nd Feb.
A.B.

Warren (N.S.W.) Herald re-opens the
death-adder-sting-tail controversy, despite “Billee
Grant’s ” yarn in a recent Bum etin. One Arthur
Murphy is alleged to have chased a death-adder
at Warren, and, when fairly cornered, the reptile
deliberately committed suicide by stinging itself
with the disputed tail fang. Three or four spec-
tators witnessed this “ rash act,” and a post-
mortem examination showed that the portion of
the body “stung” was really punctured and sur-
rounded by a green-coloured circle.

Mr. Richard Waters, Pygmalion and Galatea
Illusions, Goulburn, N.S.W., who writes on May
23rd, 1893 As a professional and one who is
continually travelling, there is a tremendous
amount of hard work attached to our occupation.
I have no doubt you, as a business man, can un-
derstand what I mean— there is a lot of mental
worry attached to our profession. I have been
running the Pygmalion and Galatea Illusions
down here on the Southern Coast; if it had not
been for the great nerve strengthening powers of
Clements Tonic, I do not believe 1 would ever
have been able to carry out my engagements. If
you think that by publishing this letter it will
benefit any of my brother professionals, you are at
liberty to do so. Wishing Clements Tonic all
kinds of success, I remain, yours truly, Richard
Waters, Pygmalion and Galatea Illusions, Goul-
burn, N.S. W.

And at Newcastle, Brisbane and Toowoomba.
daughter in his own strictly business fashion, and
the old man consented, chiefly because he valued
the bag-youth’s capabilities as a salesman. The
girl also consented, reluctantly. She saw her
bagman seldom during their engagement, because
he was always away pushing the softgoods inter-
est, but he wrote her beautiful, poetic, soulful
letters. And at last she came to love him by reason
of these letters, and decided that it was only a
natural shyness while in her company that made
him conceal his lofty character beneath a hoarse
voice and a perpetual flow of disconnected conver-
sation concerning calico. Then they were married
and presently she found that she had wedded a
bagman pure and simple. The letters had been
written by the other fellow, and the man was
only a plain, unvarnished, red-faced drummer of
the most prosaic description. That was some
fourteen years ago, and all that fourteen years
the bag-husband has come home regularly from
his warehouse, and made a brief remark or two
about the state of trade, and then eaten a large
dinner, and slumbered by fits and starts through
the evening, in company with a glass of brandy
and water. It has been a long and solemn
awakening.—Titus.
* * *
„ Wellington (M.L.) City Council, the other
day, struck the worst snag in its record. Said
Council has strong moral views concerning the
“ social evil,” and its members are mostly solid on
matters relating to the “age of consent.” But
when a councillor named Petherick rose up and
read out a list of all the eminently respectable
and pious property-holders in the city, whose
houses were let to persons of bad character at
about twice the rental which would be paid by
any person of even moderately good character,
there was a silence which could have been heard
at a distance of many miles. The Council refused
to discuss the matter, and the papers avoided all
mention of the horrible incident. Even the most
aggravated social purist draws the line when it
becomes a question of disturbing the respectable
property-holder.
# * #
The Argus sounds the tocsin of alarm re
encroachment on the Melb. public parks and
gardens. The papers did not stand by Fitz-
gibbon, when he fought in this cause, ere the
mischief was done, vested interests being too
great. He is never tired of recalling how he rode
with Victoria’s first Governor, Latrobe, round the
reserves, while Latrobe expatiated on his fore-
thought in providing these city lungs—since
chopped up right and left, through municipal
c.irruption.
# * *
According to ex-senior warder John W.
Longford, the public flagellator holds, or used to
hold, pride of place at Darlinghurst Prison. He is
chief at the gate, and has the duty, pleasure, and
honour of receiving ladies and gentlemen who visit
the establishment, even vice-royalty itself.
The pretty woman unhesitatingly follows
you into the lonely compartment. The ugly hesi-
tates, and loses—the train.

Chinese burglars at Singapore fill their
pig-tails with skewers and fish-hooks, so as to
make the police let go.
* » •
Melbourne apparently contains every
■ officer cashiered from the British army during the
past 40 years. The writer (an army man) has,
within the past four years, encountered five (pro-
fessedly) cashiered officers of 17th Lancers ; two of
the 11th Hussars; two of the 4th Dragoons (or
Dragoon Guards); one of the 10th Hussars ; two
of the Horse Guards (Blue); four of the Grenadier
'Guards; one of the Coldstream Guards; and
'between 20 and 30 officers in Line regiments.
Without exception, the introductions have cost
’the writer 3d. to ss. a time (as “temporary loans”),
and it has only just dawned on him that he did
not come to this beastly country as a peram-
bulating British pension-office.

Bear Bulletin, —Me “Hop.’s” police-
arming grotesqueries, the Dutch policemen in
Celebes carry a long-shafted, two-pronged fork
wherewith safely to pin the “ amok-runner ” by
the neck against a wall or the ground. Couldn’t
We fix up our “active and intelligent” with some-
thing in the way of an elongated trident, with
which to spear his prey afar off? He wouldn’t
skewer the wrong man any oftener than the be-
pistolled “ cop ” would shoot him ! —Macassar.
* * *
There is an ex-N.S.W. M.P. who owes
his present bad health and his exclusion from
Parliament to a worrying controversy he got into
with the Telegraph Department on the divided
word “To-day.” He would not pay for two
Words at the counter of a country office, with
the results that his wire did not go, his wife
did not come, and his daughter’s marriage was
frustrated.
Melbournian: “ They show me, in the
outskirts, an acreage ploughed by an ex-bank
manager.”

Owing to bank suspensions and their con-
sequences, Bishop Camidge, of Bathurst, who left
Australia last week on a trip to England, filled up
the higher vacancies which, since the suspensions,
occurred in his diocese, from the ranks of those
of his clergy who possessed private means.

appropriate Melb. one. For Is. a month members
get nice gravestones.

friend.—Polype.

A certain Australian
detective-office lately en-
gaged two amateur short-
hand-writers, reputed to
be very fast, to take
down the inflammatory
speeches expected to be
delivered at an unem-
ployed meeting. They
got the speeches down,
but couldn’t read their
notes next morning.
* * #
A reminder for the
ladies now crowding out
trades and professions :
When a man earns a
decent income he almost
invariably takes a wife,
and so gives a woman a
more or less permanent
billet. But a woman does
not relieve the labour-
market of a man when
she begins to rake in coin.
* # *
The majority of the
elderly police of Sydney
strongly object to the
“arming” of the police.
The majority of theyoung
constables favour it. The
present writer has been in
almost every bye-way of
Sydney, from midnight
till morn, in company
with detectives, and
maintains that if a poll
of Sydney constabulary
be taken the majority
will favour batons —not
necessarily thebatons now
in use but a weapon
which falls short of chal-
lenging an antagonist to
murder. “ Why don’t
you,” asked the present
scribe, perambulating a
lane in Woolloomooloo at
2 a.m., “carry a revol-
ver?” “ Not for my life,”
replied the detective ;
“now they lay themselves
out for a fight, but if they
believed I carried a re-
volver they’d lay me out
on sight.”

THE BLACK PREDECESSOR.
MARY THE GIN (to squatter's bride, just arrived at homestead): “You
Missus Jackson now?”
BASHFUL BRIDE: “Yes.”
MARY: “Oh, me Missus Jackson once !” —(Conversation ends here.)
Natural enough for three men to repudiate
the paternity of one infant (indeed, it’s 100 to 2on
two doing so), but when three men claim paternity
of one child matters get mixed, especially as there
is only one mother. A case arose in Blackburn
(Lancashire) two months ago where an unmarried
mother left <£2ooo to the father of her child. The
will omitted his name, hence a big law-suit. When
it comes on for hearing the Court ought to be
crowded out.

The British “National Leprosy Fund”
offer a prize of .£52 10s. for the best re-
port “on the reputed recent increase of leprosy
on the Australian continent, its extent and pos-
sible causes.” The report must be sent in on or
before Dec. 31, 1894, to No. 1 Park Crescent,
Regent’s Park, London.
A TEMPERANCE ADDRESS.
I have no rhyme to spin this week,
No verses can I make ;
The brain, and not the will, is weak ;
My head begins to ache
With every fresh attempt to find
A subject for a song—
And since the Muse is so unkind
1 know my liver’s wrong.
In vain I woo the gentle maid
With soft, appealing sighs.
I’ve pains beneath the shoulder-blade,
And bilious-looking eyes.
The Muse’s heart was not so hard
In days when I was young—
But, ah ! she spurns the hairless bard
With fur upon his tongue.
I mind the time when I could tie
A towel round my head,
And sit me down and versify
Whilst Wisdom lay a-bed.
But now the awful “glass that cheers "
Puts fancies all to rout.
I’m growing old —excuse these tears —
And sin has found me out.
Strong Drink, he is a crafty foe,
Whose guile we never gauge
Until our teeth begin to show
The signs of middle age.
Then manufacturers of verse,
With Wilfrid Denver, say—
“ O, God ! put back Thy universe
And give us yesterday.”
Dear faithful friend, repent in time —
Nay, do not gaily scoff ;
I cannot write a topic rhyme
Because my liver’s “ off.”
I had three “ goes ” of liquid flame
Which wasn’t much ; heigho !
Poor sober Now must bear the blame
For careless Long Ago.
E.F.
Alike is human nature frail,
Or be it female, bo it male.
There’s “Mona’s ” “wanted ” must be old,
Besides possessing storaged gold—
She wants to wed a played-out shape,
And dreams how she would look in crape.
The advertising male above
Hath knowledge how the sexes love ;
Before he mentions trading, mark !
He tells them he is tall and dark ;
And tall and dark men aye declare
Their hearts are fired by maids who’re fair ;
And so he runs the vogue, but, zounds !
His own reserve’s <£1000!

DOMESTIC ECONOMY !
WHA.T IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpenoe, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpence, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you ? Will you do your business at

WILLIAM JOHN LYNE.
“GOOD AD VICE AND A WOODEN DEG.”
If I hadn’t given my friend Jim Smalley the
best piece of advice one young fellow could give
another we should be friends still—that is, if Jim
could have lived without the advice. This may
sound rather strange and mixed to you, but it’s all
right when you take it by the handle.
You see it was this way. Jim was a handsome
chap, 25 years old, foppish and dressy, fond of
society, had plenty of money, but with the seeds
of consumption in him. Got ’em from his mother,
who died of it.
Well, Jim began to cough, and run down hill
fast. The doctors couldn’t help him, and told him
so. One day he was talking to me about it, and
actually broke down and cried. “Jim,” says I,
“ there’s just one chance for you, and I want you
to jump for it right away. That’s to go out West
in America and live on the slopes of the Rocky
Mountains, in the pine woods, in a tent or a hut,
and stay there till you are dead or well. Don’t
write to me for a year, then come back or let me
hear from you.”
Bidding a sad farewell to the young girl he was
engaged to be married to, Jim went.
Two years afterwards I met him in town ; he
was as hearty as a buck, bub walked with a limp.
He had lost his right leg, below the knee, in a fight
with a grizzly bear, and now hobbled around on a
wooden one. “And it’s all your fault,” he said,
“If it hadn’t been for your advice I’d never gone
there. Now Edith won’t marry me. Says she
don’t want a husband with a wooden leg, and I
don’t want a friend who gave me the wooden leg.”
Well, there ! I was never so taken aback. My
advice had saved Jim’s life and restored his health,
yet because he couldn’t have two sound legs and a
wife besides, he threw me overboard. I vowed I’d
never give anybody a bit of good advice again.
I’d let ’em die first. But that’s where I was
wrong. It is a man’s duty to keep on doing
good, whether people are grateful or not. Here is
Mr. Frank Stanley Langman. His wife gave him
a piece of good advice, and he was sensible enough
to act on it. In June, 1882, it was that he fell ill.
He felt weak, tired and weary without any outside
reason for it. His appetite was poor, there was a
bitter taste in his mouth, and a bad pain in the
chest and stomach after eating. Sometimes he
would break out into a sweat and feel so pros-
trated he’d have to lie down. It was feared he
had some kind of internal tumour. Once he had
an attack at the railway station, and people
crowded round him, thinking he was dying.
During another attack he kissed his child, be-
lieving his time had come. A doctor examined
him for heart disease, but couldn’t find any. He
ad vised Langman to take only milk and brandy,
milk and water, and such slops. Still he had
those frightful periodic attacks. After attending
him some time, the doctor said, “ I can’t find out
what is the matter with you ; you had better see a
West End physician.” Mr. Langman did so, and
the West End doctor said the patient’s liver made
too much bile, and ordered medicine and a milk
diet.
Two more doctors were consulted with no better
result, and the unhappy man remained in that
same miserable form for seven years. In Feb-
ruary, 1889, he read in a newspaper of a case like
his own having been cured by Mother Seigel’a
Curative Syrup, but, inasmuch as the best medical
advice in London was of no use, what could be
expected from an advertised medicine? “Noth-
ing, of course,” said Mr. Langman.
His wife thought differently. “ Fom try Seigel’s
Syrup,” she said, “ Everybody speaks well of it.”
He did try it, and in three months he was well,
and has been well ever since. In a letter dated
December 17, 1891, he says, “ Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup saved my life,’’ and signs his
name to what he says—“ Frank Stanley Lang-
man, 44, Comberford Road, Brockley.”
His malady was not heart disease or tumors,
but indigestion and dyspepsia, the cause of almost
all pangs and pains, call them what you will.
Mr. Langman was saved by good advice and a
good medicine, for which he is grateful. So I
take notice that everybody isn’t like Jim Smalley,
witii his grizzly bear and his wooden leg.”
London, February, 1892.
G.W.C.
“Have you anything to say before we
eat you?” said the King of the Cannibal Isles to
a Boston missionary. “I have,” was the reply ;
“I want to talk to you awhile on the advantages
of a vegetable diet.”— Bazar.
Newcastle (N.S.W.) School of Arts decides
by 37 votes to 34 not to open the reading-room on
Sundays. The parsons fought tooth and nail
against the change. But they can’t fight against
the future. Time is on the side of the 34.
Bendigo hospital now supplies Bendigo-
manufactured brandy to its patients. Good. And
the British Army medical authorities have decided
to use Victorian brandy in military hospitals. The
triumphant hour of Australian wines becomes
more visible every day.
Tasmania has an Art Society, and the Art
Society has had an exhibition, and the exhibition
was opened by Gov. Gormanston, and Gov. Gor-
manston has said that Tasmania is making great
artistic strides, and on the subject of strides Gov.
Gormanston is an authority, seeing his own
measures 48 inches. On the subject of art his
dictum is questioned ; and one critic, Montague
Rhys-Jones, erstwhile of Sydney, goes so far as to
say that Tasmanian art consists almost entirely of
hideous daubs. This latter pronouncement has
caused a terrific sensation locally. Everybody in
Tasmania has a hobby. Many people hunt what
the Yankees call “bugs,” including snakes for
bottling purposes; and nearly everyone is a
musician and an artist. The life of Montague
Rhys-. Tones is therefore quite unsafe in Hobart,
and he had better take to the Lake country.
Of 28,294 women on the roll in the chief
cities (Auckland, Wellington, Christchurch, and
Dunedin) at the Maoriland Parliamentary general
election, 23,724 voted ! In New Plymouth, 1289
women voted out of 1465 enrolled, and in Wan-
ganui 1454 out of 1660; Wellington suburbs, 1622
out of 1860. And so it was all round. “Do
women want the franchise ? ” It seems so.
The story of Cyril Haviland, ex-secretary
of Sydney School of Arts, just sentenced to four
years’ gaol for embezzlement, is that of a man who
went to wreck through too much enthusiasm,
Haviland, who was, originally at least, a well-
meaning fellow, lived in a constant state of
enthusiasm. He had a first-class position in the
N.S.W. Civil Service —but threw it up to pursue
spooks and ghosts and elementary spirits and
materialisations of the dead, and other clammy
visitants from over the border. He had a hand-
some fortune, and he spent most of it in publish-
ing alleged poetry of his own and literature which
nobody read, and scattered the rest on the red-
nosed “medium ” who breathes a fragrance of gin
and puts one in communication with the unsub-
stantial wraith of his grandmother. When he
became attached to the School of Arts he started
out with the same deranged enthusiasm as before.
He worked day and night with all his heart and
soul, got women’s subscriptions reduced, filled the
mouldering institution with new subscribers, tore
things to pieces and built them up again, and pub-
lished a paper which nobody read to advertise the
place and make it boom more than ever. The one
thing he could not do was to make the hall a
source of revenue, and, with his usual enthu-
siasm, he secretly took it (or a share in
it) himself at a rental which was a good
deal more than twice the amount of his
salary. Then, with this white elephant on his
hands, he stole the funds of the institution to run
or help run the show with. Also, a vegetarian-
restaurant fad of his seems to have run away with
some of his employers’ money. Most of his steal-
ings seem to have gone in loose, mad enthusiasms
of one kind and another. His fate is a proof that
some kind of asylum is badly wanted for men
with twitching nerves and jumpy muscles, who
have a far away look in their eyes, and talk aloud
to themselves as they hop unevenly along the
street, and stop suddenly in the midst of a con-
versation to listen to the voices of the dead.
Since tobacco was first introduced inEurope,
at least in England, about 300 years ago, its
progress has been incessant, like that of coffee and
tea. Up to the last century a pipe was a luxury
reserved only for the so-called “better classes,” as
it was so dear that for half-a-crown people only got
in tobacco the weight of that coin in silver.
Times have undoubtedly changed for the better in
this respect, since in our days any tobacconist will
give you a packet of Frossard’s Swiss Cigars for a
similar piece of money.
Biggest alligators in the world live in the
Johnstone River (N.Q.). They are to other alli-
gators what bulldogs are to fleas, and when they
yawn a gale of wind levels half the sugar-cane on
the neighbouring plantations. They are very
exclusive-cut even the Chinamen dead when they
meet ’em. Their robust health has a queer
source. A few years ago a plantation punt was
being taken up the Johnstone when it capsized,
and punt, cargo, and Kanakas disappeared.
Before that the alligators had been no better than
those anywhere else, but they began to grow big
and strong from that day. The punt was loaded
with Parsons’ Flaked Oatmeal.
The N.S.W. Fresh Food and Ice Company
has made special preparations for the Lenten
season, and its stock of fish will be unusually
large and choice. All the best Australian and
Maoriland fish are stocked, and a shipment of fine
English fish will arrive by R.M.S. Ophir on 23rd
Feb. The unequalled facilities which this com-
pany has for freezing and preserving fish, game,
&c., ensure that quality remains always first-class,
and orders from country townships or the neigh-
bouring provinces can be promptly filled at the
lowest rates. Milk (now reduced to 3d. per
quart), butter, and all dairy products are also sup-
plied by the N.S.W. Fresh Food and Ice Co.
Premier Dibbs and the Parsons.
Despite his frequent intermittent, public,
bizarre applications of texts of holy writ, when
endeavouring to confound his enemies, N.S.W.
Premier Dibbs must possess a big share of sar-
donic humour. With what infantile assumption
he played last week’s deputation of parsons, and
what a graceless and extraordinary sinner those
neuter-genders must have thought him ! To the
mind tha,t can picture the scene, the whole affair
is a shriek of well-controlled humbug. “We
represent the Council of the Churches.” “ What
is this Council of the Churches ?” Then, with the
oily impressiveness of the saints whose foot-
steps make the foundations of Sheol quiver, it
was told Dibbs that the Council of the
Churches represented “ the six largest Protes-
tant denominations of the province—Anglicans,
Presbyterians, Wesleyans, Congregationalists,
Baptists, and Primitive Methodists.” Dibbs
never turned _a hair. The parsons wanted the
Sunday aquariums and the Tivoli closed up on
Sundays, but could only “get in with their left”
by saying that those institutions made charges.
“Where is this Tivoli ?” asked Dibbs, with new-
chum curiosity. The chief deputationist didn’t
know, but several others did. “Which of you
was there last ?” was the Premier’s next innocent,
baby-like query. “Oh, we don’t go to such
places! The entertainments are secular.” “What
do you_ call secular ?’’ cried Dibbs, with the no-
savee air of a Chinaman.
Parson Walker: They are by no means sacred in any
form. They are comic. And we have evidence that they
sing parodies of sacred songs.
Dibhs: Have you been able to draw the line between
secular and sacred music? I have made several attempts
and failed.
Some further conversation and Dibbs blurted:
—“The paying seems to be the crux of the
business and screwed out of the cleric that the
parsons had no objection to let these secular show's
take up collections and depend on them.
Dibbs: You all take up a collection yourselves. You
send the hat round, every one of you. If you had to
depend on a collection, you would have to close, too.
Walker; Oh, no, we shouldn’t. We are satisfied to
depend on a collection.
In his enthusiasm for the cause, Parson Walker
evidently forgot momentarily that he enjoys an
assured salary, independently of collections. On
his complaining that the Tivoli doings are a
‘desecration of the British Sunday” :
Dibbs : What is the British Sunday? I have heard of
British heer, but not of British Sunday.
Walker: I think you know what it is.
Dibbs; I don’t know what it is. Do you go to the two
tables of stone for it ?
Walker: Yes.
Dibbs: Then you are out of court You clergy,
you know, are too exacting; and, because you are too
exacting, the people are obliged to resort to these tricks.
But the Parthian shot the bald-headed and spec-
tacled old pelican delivered was perhaps the most
delicious bit of the lot (italics ours): —
Dibbs: At the same time, I tell you this, that, pious
Christians as we all are, when we start to straightlace the
Sabbath too much, we do more harm than good.
A fancy portrait of Dibbs straightlacing the
Sabbath would sell like tote-tickets.

astounding revelations from Mr. Wynne’s diary.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Ned Hanlan, the aquatic light of other days,
clings to his profession in a way painful to behold. Ten
years, or thereabouts, have slipped away since Beach
first rowed him to death, and still he goes on issuing
challenges, and swaggering around in the rags of his
reputation. There never was a sadder case than Hanlan
of a man being unable to do more than one thing. He
can’t turn his hands to anything except sculls, or under-
stand that a mildewed ex-champion, having no young
admirers, loses his interest for the old ones if he stays on
to get beaten by all and sundry. There will soon be
nobody to raise a subscription for the poor extinct
volcano. The latest farce arranged is a double sculling
match on the Thames between Hanlan and Gaudaur
(another of Beach’s ancient victims) in the one boat, and
Sullivan and Harding in the other. Mainly a gate-money
affair on the face of it. Two or three steamboats follow
the race, a few jugginses pay 10s. or £1 for places, and a
vast number of thieves buy tickets on the chance of being
able to plunder the jugginses aforesaid. An odd dozen
or two of demented rowing club boobies may buy the
“colours” of their fancy pair at fancy prices, and
perhaps some riverside-pub. keepers allow a commission
on their extra business. That’s about the size of the
whole matter.
• * *
It is said Yankee sculler Petersen is coming
over to row Stanbury on the Parramatta. Sculling is too
dead in Australia to allow of the advent of a worn-out
third-rater reviving it.
• • *
N.S. Wales “ Billy ” Macpherson’s phenomenal
times at the Australian championship meeting have drawn
the attention of the London papers to Australian amateur
athletics.
* # •
Mr. “Syd.” Walford, whose weekly big scores
during past and present season caused some controversy
as to his claim for a place in theN.S.W. provincial eleven,
has been a failure with the bat during the Maoriland
tour.
* * •
Joe Choynski and Steve O’Donnell have been
matched in the States. Steve is a wonderfully clever
boxer, but the tricky Joseph will perhaps show too much
“devil " for the Sydneyite.
* » #
Tiiree months’ tote fines in Victoria realised
£1800. More than £138 a week.
# # #
The complaints of J. B. “ Clark” against handi-
capper Dakin will set people wondering how some of Mr.
Clark s horses, notably Camoola and Autonomy, could
be satisfactorily handicapped upon their form in weight-
for-age races.
# • •
The selection committee has picked the follow-
ing 16 oarsmen from whom to choose eight to represent
N.S.\V. in the coming Interprovincial Eight-oar Race:—
Moor, Waters, Ramsay, M'Murdo, Laing, Kennedy,
Cadell, Aldworlh, Pearson, Cox, Astridge, Williams,
Parkinson, Goulding, Higgs, and Keenan. To commence
with, Aldworth, Pearson, and Cox may be put on one
side as unworthy of consideration. In picking an eight-
oar crew more than individual ability has to be taken into
count, and, taking one consideration with another, the
writer would like to see the province represented thus :—
Stroke Side.
st. lb.
Laing (2) 11 4 N.S.R.O.
Keenan (4) 116 E.S.R.C.
Waters (6) 12 4 N.S.R.C.
Moor (str.) 10 6 N.S.R.C.
45 6
Bow Side.
st. lb.
M‘Donald(b) 10 8 M.R.O.
M'Murdo (3) 11 6 N.S.R.C.
Kennedy (5) 12 6 S.R.O.
Berokelm’n(7)10 8 N.S.R.C.
45 0
The above crew is evenly weighted and composed from
first to last of sterling performers. No doubt a deal of
club jealousy would be caused by the inclusion of five
North Shore R.C. men, but the fact that the North Shore
club has, for a couple of seasons, simply walked away with
all the senior races justifies it. Berckehnan, w ho is not
included in the 16, is the next man after Moor the writer
wou;d pick if selecting an eight, as a good No. 7 is even
harder than a stroke to come across ; and Berckelman
has shown his worth in rowing No. 7 to Moor’s stroke in
the eight-oar victories of the North Shore club ; in addi-
tion to being in five winning senior four-oar crews in
succession. As a matter of fact, the North Shore club,
and the North S tore club only, has dropped across the
correct style of getting an eight through the water, and
if the only consideration was the winning of the Inter-
provinoial race by N.S.W., the victorious North Shore
crew would be picked upon holus-bolus to represent the
province.
• # *
The Hawkesbury Racing Club is declining fast.
This is a pity, as every penny taken at the gate is devoted
to the sport. The proprietary clubs which have lately
sprung up around Sydney have killed the old-fashioned
provincial meetings which did so much for the turf.
* • •
Dooley, of Sydney, is matched against Laing.
Taking the line through Goddard, the half-caste should
win. Then Dooley, as regards condition, seems to have
gone over the line which cannot be reorossed. Better for
him to fight with all his fat on than try to get into trim in
a few weeks. But he is one of those nervous men who
show best in an uphill fight where little is expected from
them.
• • #
Carnage may be a better horse than Carbine
was at the same age, for Donald Wallace’s, or rather Dan
O'Brien’s, colt went down before Ensign in the V.ll.O.
Derby, hut atoned for defeat a few days after. Still, it
must he remembered that Carbine trained on, whereas
Carnage, like many others, may decline at four or five.
No horse, at the end of his three-year-old career, had a
belter record than Trident, hut it fell to pieces when he
met the Australian Peer the following spring. Then, as
three-year-olds. The Peer was Abercorn’s equal, if not
superior, hut afterwards there was no comparison.
# # •
Australian boxers must have abroad a reputa-
tion for straightness which they do not invariably enjoy
when at borne. Both Mitchell and Corbett were attended
in the ring bv men from the land of the kangaroo.
» # •
There is talk about forming a second Tatt.’s in
Sydney. The present club proposes to debar members
from transacting business at tobacconists’ shops, &c.,
which now come into competition with the “rooms.”
* * *
Pedestrianism in Melbourne is looking up, but
it can never attain the glory it did in Sydney when
Botany, Carrington and half-a-dozen other grounds were
in full blast.
* » •
Sailor Prince is said to be the most improved
horse at Flemington, and to have thickened wonderfully
since the spring. Admirers of Carnage may do well in
noting the report.
* * *
We have several starting machines. But tjie
machine most wanted is one to keep the prads going
after they do start.
# # #
Sheffield (Eng.) City Council has decided to
obliterate all the sporting news in the newspapers sup-
plied to the municipal libraries. They don’t say anything
about Stock Exchange quotations. More motcy lias been
lost and more men ruined on the Stock Exchange during
til',' last ten years than on tlie turf in twice that space of
time. O.i the turf a man lias occasionally a look in, but
In Capel Court, never.
It grieves Broken Hill Wilson to learn that the
public are a lot more anxious about the health of Carnage
than they are of his own. Why not? A small percentage
of the public has benefited by (backing) Carnage, but who
has grown fat through Mr. Wilson?
• » •
Perhaps the closest and most exciting finish yet
seen in Tasmania was at the Hobart Steeple (15th inst.).
Jack in the Green first, Foxhall second, and Reckless, half
a head from first horse, third. The majority of the public
present, however, considered Foxhall the winner. A close
finish after a 2' s mile go.
• « •
The owner of a horse—and a good gee-gee, too
—running on the proprietary tracks about Sydney, is a
very old of a Sydney mercantile house.
# * •
The programme of the N.S. Wales Agricultural
Show requires revision. There is not a single competition
for that most useful sized horse, the galloway.
* * »
The “gentleman jock.” is as hard to handle as
the professional article. Not long since, in a Corinthian
race near Sydney, an owner decided to keep his horse
for a future Saturday, and gave the “gentleman” rider
orders, at the last moment, to run a bye. “Too late,”
quoth the gent., “I’ve backed the horse to win me a
nice httle stake, and I’m going to ride for all he’s
worth.” The owner only just had time to rush back to
the paddock and get his punter to put the stuff on.
* * *
At Melbourne, last week, Australian cycling
records were made : 5 miles, L. B. Scharp, 12 min. 46 sec.;
5 miles tandem, White Brothers, 12 min. 37 l-sth sec.;
half-mile, A. Turner, 1 min. 7 3-sth sec.; 2 miles, M‘Combe,
4 min. 56 2-sth sec.
# # *
Some owners appear privileged to run the most
barefaced byes on the Sydney proprietary courses, but, as
recent events show, woe betide the unfortunate countryite
if he try a bit of cunning business.
* * *
Three newly-elected N.S.W. Mayors are, or
have been, bookmakers. Is this a typical forecast of the
“sound Christian” representatives in Parliament that
the goody-goo dy leaders are trumpeting for ?
* * *
Though it may interfere with the Distressed
Jockeys’ Fund, the patent starting-machine is likely to
stay. It will save much bad language and many charges
of favouritism, dishonesty, and ineompetency against the
man with the flag. It is absurd to find starters fining
jockey-boys for bad language, while the boss starter
indulges in eloquence which would make a bullock-driver
turn green.
* * *
The meeting of Jackson and Corbett will put
the world’s pug championship in a more satisfactory posi-
tion than it has been in for many a day.
Denying that four lines of doggerel related
to a councillor, a Vic. shire engineer wrote :
If the above statement is not true, may the Almighty
inflict such punishment on me as may deprive me of my
sight, speech, and torture me with the severest pains and
most loathsome diseases to the day of my death.
Cabled that the Germans have been flog-
ging the wives of their native soldiers at the
Cameroons (Africa) for alleged laziness. Which
once more recalls to The Bulletin the fat Ger-
man gentleman it saw some years ago sitting on a
fence in the Wimmera, sucking a big pipe, while
his wife followed the plough, said lady being
attired chiefly in a pair of Wellington boots.
N.S.W, Government proposes to take the
Sydney Outer Domain from the control of
Botanical Gardens curator Moore, and vest it in
trustees as a “ park.” Which would prevent the
holding, “save with the trustees’ consent,” of
public meetings there (a public meeting being de-
fined as one consisting of over 10 persons)
and thus block the big Sunday gatherings
of Socialists and others. If the bull-headed
Dibbs wants to infuriate about 20,000 of the
most aggressive and earnest citizens, whose
ideas of Sunday worship take the form
which he is going to abolish, he will go right ahead
with his scheme. He will very soon discover that
even he can’t safely sweep away in one autocratic
act the Sydney Sunday Fair.
Messrs. Haworth, cutlers, of 401 George-sfc.,
Sydney, are doing a large business with the
“Christy bread-knife,” a weapon with a serrated
edge, like a saw or the outer rim of a streak of
lightning. Its special advantage is that it goes
through last week’s stale crust when the usual im-
plement gives up in disgust; that it easily cuts
new bread when the ordinary knife merely bashes
the loaf in some horrifying shape without pene-
trating its dank, tough surface ; and that it does
its work cleanly, unlike the common weapon of
commerce which scatters crumbs over the pre.
mises from the cellar right up to the chimney-top.
All the rage in Paris. Send 13 penny
stamps for latest Parisian preventative. National
Drug Cos., Russell-st., Melbourne.
Ernest Knox, duly black-capped for having
while burgling killed a Vic. pawnbroker’s son,
seems to think that his life will be spared. He is
a most credulous young man.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to, if you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,

A Hymn of the Time.
Where the blue of the Palestine hills casts a shade
o’er the grey of the plain,
Bloody men did a deed that has cursed them for
all the ages to be ;
And, now, in the light of an age herculean, they
slay Christ again—
They slay Him by slaying the poor—by enslaving
the men who were free—
And for naught has Christ died, for the hucksters
who clothed His old temples with shame,
Now spit on his name.
Oh ! Christ, ere a year’s overpast —ere the year
that’s to be is begun
We pray Thee deliver the many who toil ’neath
the whips of the few ;
Burn them, oh Lord, with a flame from a red and
ineffable sun,
They who have stolen the land, and the rains,
and the sea, and the dew ;
Help us, oh ! Many-Times-Slain—distant God,
whom we see through a mist —
Help us, oh Christ!
GEORGE AUSTIN.
