Australia is now supplying one-thirteenth
of tho total wine consumption of the United
Kingdom. With the exception of France, Spain,
and Portugal, we have passed all competitors, and
our imports into the United Kingdom are fourth
on the list in magnitude.
Foreign critics of Victorian morality will
be glad to get a full report of tho last Gillies
oration. Gillies called them to account, and dis-
posed of their most cruel insinuations with one of
Ids oldest “gags.” It was left for the shiny little
man to restore confidence by accident, for although
he doubtless didn’t intend his remark to apply
to Victoria it is full of comfort all the same.
“ If,” said Gillies, “ we could only induce some of
those gentlemen who have spoken not wisely but
too well, to come here and be told that the colony
was only a little over GO years old—if they could
be shown what the colony had been able to do in
half-a-century, they would be amazed. Some of
them might have been a little hurt by specula-
tions ; but justice was justice notwithstanding
(hear, hear).” A Melbourne bard chants :
A REVELATION.
Our Duncan has said it!
Delighted we read it,
Good news for our credit
Which down in the dust is!
The Law, lately fiouted,
No more shall be doubted,
Suspicion is routed
By “Justice is justice.”
For senators lushing,
All bent upon hushing
Up evidence crushing,
Still strong our disgust is ;
But “pressure” and clatter
(Though Fat Man were falter)
Can’t silence the matter
When Justice is justice.
The Truth, sole and wholly,
For high as for lowly !
We’ll keep the Law holy ;
The boomer w ho bust is,
If guilty of “ hanky ”
In transactions hanky,
Will scarcely any, “ thankee”
When justice is justice.
An end to concealing
Of dark double-dealing,
Now scorn is congealing,
For simple our trust is
In Duncan true-hearted,
Who, ere he departed,
The secret imparted
That justice is justice.
Editor Bulletin, — Centipedes do not
swallow their young. I often turn up broods of
them, sometimes as many as seventeen young in
one clutch. The mother usually gathers up her
family one at a time in the month, passes them
underneath her, until she has them all stowed
away between her legs; then makes for cover.—
John Loader, Edinglassie, Muswellbrook,
N.S.W., Feb. 9, ’94.
Sydney Truth publishes every week a
great slab of an alleged serial described as
“Thirty years reminiscences of Sydney” (copy-
right reserved !), by that much-advertised person,
Timothy Fogarty. Timothy is apparently the
most incurably serious and long-winded man in
N.S.W. A specimen of his “reminiscences”
At the time of my arrival, firewood was brought into
Sydney on wood-carts for sale. The wood was generally
ironbork trees, cut into lengths of about eight or ten feet,
and sold in different parts of the town, blear the Court
House at Darlinghurst, was a great stand—this supply
was generally purchased by the residents of Surry Hills.
A load, say about a ton of th's wood, would cost from
15s. to 20s., according to the supply, but it afterwards
would require to be cut in lengths. Miller and I pur-
chased a large load each, and being strong, vigorous men,
set to work cutting the wood. I borrowed a hand-saw
from my landlord, a German, a good-hearted, jolly fellow,
who laughed heartily when he saw me attacking the job,
but, like a sensible man, he encouraged me. I worked
away, the perspiration teeming off me, and before one
o’clock my hand was quite blistered from holding and
forcing the saw through the wood. When I cooled suffi-
ciently I had a bath ; the bath I constructed out of a
packing case, zinc-lined. Very few houses except large
ones were'supplied with baths in those days. I out
' a hole in the 1 bottom for the water to run out. The
bath was placed in a small wooden room in the
yard, and supplied from the water-tap by a piece of gutta-
percha tubing, and proved a most serviceable article for
the family. After dinner I went over to see my friend ;
Miller was working away, the perspiration teeming off
him, but, unlike me, his hand was not blistered, although
he was using a hand saw: he took the precaution to put
on a strong leather glove. I told him to knock off, and as
soon as he could get ready we would go into town and
purchase a cross-cut saw. This he did, and in about one
hour we were on our way into town and purchased the saw
which I brought home. He promised to be with me in
the morning at six o’clock, when we would set to and cut
my load of wood into short lengths. Miller was over at
time named ; the saw worked well, we were delighted,
and by dinner time we nearly had the wood finished. My
wife arranged to have Mrs. Miller and the children to
spend the evening with us. By four o’clock the wood
was cut and stacked in the shed. Mr. Miller returned
home to change his clothes, and at six o’clock that even-
ing both families sat down to tea as happy as they
possibly could be. Our good wives praising their hus-
bands in turn for the work accomplished, and many a
hearty laugh had we over the wood-cutting. Next day I
went over to help Mr. Miller to cut his wood, and my wife
and children came over in the evening to tea. The wood
being cut, a happy evening was spent with Mrs. Miller
and her family.
It is a gladsome thing to hear that a man made a
hole in the bottom of a zinc case, 30 years ago, so
that the water might run out. And the horror of
it is that he doesn’t mention what he stopped
the hole with while he was standing up in the
case with nothing on him except soap, and wash-
ing himself away back in 1864. This omission
unsettles the reader’s mind. Also, he doesn’t
state whether he dried himself with a towel or
merely stood around until ho evaporated, which
is another dismal gap in the narrative.
Nervous sufferers will line! it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advb. on page
17 of this issue.

The survivors of the crew of the coasting
steamer, Kanahooka, wrecked three weeks ago in
the Gulf of Carpentaria, had a particularly rough
time of it. They left the ship in an open boat,
18ft. long, with only two oars. They drifted for
two nights and a day before the wind and sea,
when they struck the coast. The boat capsized
nearly a mile from the shore, on to which it
drifted. Two of the men were unable to
but landed with the assistance of the others. The
party then started to walk towards the Batavia
River. They travelled some miles when the route
became blocked by impenetrable scrubs. T hey
were compelled to return to the boat, and found
that the natives had stolen oars and rowlocks. Tor
days the party kept traversing from the boat to
the scrub, a distance of nearly 15 miles, in the
hope of getting through the scrub. They had
nothing to eat but sand-crabs and berries. Y- om j
of the berries nearly killed them. The men lived
on little else but water, while they dug holes and
partially buried themselves at night as protection
from the mosquitoes and sand-flies. It was
arranged that the second engineer and the second
mate, who were the strongest swimmers in the
party, should push on ahead. This they did, and
after severe trials, swimming the river, dodging
alligators, living on raw beche-de-mer, rotten
anples, rotten cocoanuts and fresh oocoanuts,
which were washed ashore, they reached the
Batavia River mission station. They have since
been brought to Thursday Island.
Dear Bulletin, —After much careful search
for a weak spot in your “missing word” arrange-
ment I have found what may be a weak spot.
“The missing word is placed in a sealed envelope,
&c., and the contents are known only to the
manager of this paper.” Now, I take it, that as
the manager who receives guesses as they arrive is
in a position to recognise their incorrectness at a
glance there may be a possibility of people in the
office getting an occasional inkling of a wrong
word, whereby the chance of somebody finding
the right word might be improved. Without
going further into idle speculations concerning the
manager’s system of treating coupons, I will
challenge your statement that “ every precaution
has been taken to secure absolute secrecy and
fairness.” You can point to the months when
nobody guessed the word as a proof of absolute
secrecy, it is true. All the same, I submit that
there is some possibility of the secrecy not being
absolute. A mesmerist may get hold of the
manager. Why should he know the word which is
placed in a sealed envelope ? Quite unnecessary.
I suggest that the manager write, say, 20 suitable
words on 20 slips of paper and fold them as though
he were conducting a sweep. Twenty words would
be equal to all the available words in the language,
for ten minutes after writing them he will he rni-
able to remember which he selected or which he
didn’t. Then the manager picks one of his folded
slips of paper, encloses it in the envelope, and
burns the other 19 slips. Nobody knows the
missing word.—F.

Professor Thulinius.—My Dear Sir,-1 feel.l should be want-
ing in duty, did I neglect the opportunity of sending you my
testimony as to the wondrous efficacy of your rheumatic
specific. For the past ten years I have been a, complete
martyr to rheumatism in all its forms. I have tried doctors
. . , with a view of effecting a cure tor this most trying
disease 1 am well known, and scores can testify
that at best 1 could only crawl about with difficulty. . . .
After leaving Little Bay Hospital at the close of last year 1
was persuaded to try a bottle of your “ Thulinius, and in a
week or so afterwards I could get about fairly well. Upon
again suffering a relapse last February, I took another bottle,
with an equally successful result; but at last I determined to
go through a course of treatment, and I have just completed
my fourth bottle, with the gratifying knowledge l hat I am
quite free from pain, can walk any reasonable distance with-
out fatigue, have an excellent appetite, and feel myself again
competent to re-enter my profession, added to which my oft-
repeated attacks of pleurisy have become a thing of the past.
I have not dieted myself in any way, so all the credit is due to
your wonderful remedy, under Providence, that my blood is
becoming purer daily, and its effect upon my liver and kid-
neys most pronoqnced. ... I shall only be too glad
personally to convince fellow-sufferers of the benefits to be
derived from a course of your treatment, as exemplified in
my own case, after ten years’ martyrdom. 1 remain, yours
faithfully, VV. H. NEWTON NEWMAN (Teacher of Elocution
and twenty-five years Actor and Manager),

Moonshine.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It looked to all appearance like the ending of the
night,
Although ’twas only moonshine that had flushed
the east with light,
Time’s signals seemed to signify the night had
had it’s day,
And yet the night was only turning prematurely
grey.
A bold, impulsive rooster thought the night was
almost gone,
Cocksure he saw approaching day he hailed it as
the dawn,
He flapped his wings with conscious strength, then
trumpeted in pride,
And heard his challenge echoed over all the
country-side.
His brethren took the chorus up and kept it up
until
The moon at last, with smiling face, peeped over
Bunkum Hill,
And painful was the silence then, for shame their
cackle hushed,
The cock that crowed the loudest felt the most
severely crushed. /
And mortal men like this mistake the moonshine
for the dawn,
And one man’s self-assurance leads the doubtful
many on.
We credit “ spouters ” if they say, with emphasis
and style,
“Behold! the day is dawning,” when it’s moon-
shine all the while.
Melbourne.
EIFFA.
A woman escaped from the lunatic asylum
at Ballarat the other day, and was re-captured on
the road to Melbourne, She said she wanted to
see Duncan Gillies before he left for England, to
give him a letter of introduction to her aunt in
London. Only a mad woman would do a thing
like that.
Newspaper epitaph on a recent Sydney
suicide: “She was found dead Joy two bailiffs.”
Verily, there are many strange chances in this
world, and nobody knows what strange end he
may come to, or what strange people he may be
found dead by, or what Chinaman may attend
his funeral. After a life of hard work and ill-
fortune, to die alone, by poison, in a miserable
shanty with the rent overdue, and to have one’s
remains discovered by two bailiffs !
The old Kearsage, which sank the Ala-
bama off Cherbourg on June 19, 1864, has, it is
cabled, ended her days by going down in the
Caribbean Sea, Although but a weakly-built
wooden vessel, she has been kept constantly in
commission all over the world since the day of the
memorable fight. Previous to the engagement,
Thornton, Captain Winslow’s executive officer,
suggested to him the idea of tricing her cables up
in layers outside the hull amidships so as to pro-
tect the engines. This was done, in a hurried but
effective manner, and not a shot penetrated that
portion covered by the cable-armour. Had one
done so, a different story would have been told.
John W. Longford, “for many years
senior Warder and recorder at Darlinghurst Gaol,”
but “disrated for a very; trivial error of judg-
ment,” is turning his experience to account in a
16 pp. Bvo, pamphlet, called “ Under Lock and
Key,” to be published serially. The first number
is tilled with threats of awful exposures of Dar-
linghurst mismanagement, but there is nothing
alarming so far, and the introductory eight pages
are dull. Later on come some excellent stories,
which are none the worse for being true:—
The late John Lovett, who rose from warder to b e
governor of Darlinghurst, dearly loved a joke. One day
a friend asked him why he never saw him at Randwick
races. “ Randwick! " replied Lovett, in his well-known
drawling tone, “when I go away from here I like to have 1
a holiday—-a real holiday. I did go to Randwick once, 1
years ago, but I saw so many familiar faces that I couldn’t
help thinking I was on duly all the time 1 "
At Darlinghurst everybody has a nickname.
Here are some prisoners’ titles for the warders :
Long J., Jimmy the Bull, the Racehorse, Lobengula,
Mad Mick, Mickey, the Pig, Drop me a Line, Stumpy J.,
Cough Mixture, German Charlie, Big Liz, Napoleon*
Jimmy the Whisperer, Lord Harris, Straight Edge,
Exactly So, Lobster Face, the Crying Warder, Porky, Dry
Plate, the Braidwood Terrier, Gertie the Gardener, and
Bucky the Marine.
This is a Dubbo Circuit story ;
The calendar was a very heavy one, and there were
several leading barristers up from Sydney, the late David
Buchanan among them. All the accused were provided
with counsel, except one, a hoary-headed old man,
accused of a serious offence. While preliminaries were
being arranged, this individual suddenly stood up and,
addressing the Judge, said: “ Excuse me, sir, but can’t I
have one of these things? (pointing to the bewigged
barristers.) All the others have got one." Counsel were
horrified at this vulgar man, and of course, none offered
to defend him. He had to take his chance, and it hap-
pened, oddly enough, that of all the prisoners who stood
at the bar during the whole sitting he was the only one
acquitted.
On another occasion, in a country town where the
inhabitants were notorious sheep-stealers :
“ Gentlemen of the jury,” said the Judge’s Associate,
“ you will, when your names are called, please take your
seats in the usual places,” and forthwith the first mau
walked into the. dock followed by a second, before the
horrified officials could interpose.
“ Under Lock and Key ” is published by A. A.
Murray, 111 Elizabeth-street, and has on the cover
a picture of a ragged Salvation Army captain
about to fire a volley.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “Cannalin” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.

WALTZ MUSIC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN].
Round and round,
As the world swings, so swing we,
Planets on one course set free.
Come what may, this hour has found
My heart beating close to thee.
Circle on,
Faint not, fair feet silken-shod.
That upon my heart have trod,
Limbs I’d die to gaze upon,
Circle still, my sun, my god !
Long, how long
I have waited till this hour
Bring my life to perfect flower,
I have suffered scorn and wrong,
Fled, yet could not flee thy power.
If I {?o,
Empty is the world beyond;
Dead as if a wizard’s wand
Wrought a moon-cold waste of snow
Where her face shines not, fierce or fond.
If in dreams
I behold her, she who keeps
Love a slave that laughs or weeps,
Life a glass of swift sand-streams,
Time a chasm Hope o’erleaps—
If my face, . .
Pressed to hers, makes sleep divine,
As a draught of charmed wine
(Though not mine that happy place.
And that pillow sweet not mine) —
Yet the ghost
Of the rapture that I have nob
Far outrides the lips I crave not
Of the rose-crowned maiden host—
Lips whose kisses lose not, save not.
If I wake
To her cold and careless glance,
There’s a warm, white breast the'dance
Stirs to panting breaths that shake
All the outer walls of chance.
White is she.
White as snow, as fire is white.
As a star-flame in the night,
Only burning cold to_me,
Scorching to a nearer sight.
If I span
With my arm that slender shape.
In the dance there’s no escape ;
Let her fly me if she can,
Though the gulf of Judgment gape!
Near, so near,
Wheeling thus, I breathe her breath,
And the earth whirls underneath
With a joy as deep as fear,
Awful as the kiss of death.
Oh, my own !
(Since one moment with my arm
I may hold thee as a charm,
Call thy beauty mine alone,
Circle, circling thee from harm.)
Tirae that flies
Shall not give this hour again ;
Love that wanes, as moons that wane,
Faints not ever in thine eyes
If thy breast his head disdain.
From thy scorn
He may seek some refuge still
Over valley, over hill,
Through the night and on to morn—•
And thy day-break lone and chill.
Then in vain—
Vain to call him, name on name,
With wide eyes and lips aflame—
Shall to-night return again
'To morrow night and be the same ?
Nay, be wise!
Dear brown eyes that gaze as though
Slumbered still the spirit’s glow
Ere its sun of love arise—
Who shall ever love thee so ?
Better now,
Now to take a whole life’s gift
Than to wait with watchful thrift
And a white-wreathed maiden brow,
As the slow days death ward drift.
On thy breast
Pale tube-roses faint are drooping;
I will give thee for thy stooping
Orange flowers of golden crest
In white robes as angels grouping.
Bronze-brown hair!
They shall wreathe thee, crown thee, make
Monarch of thee for my sake !
Thou a woman art, and fair;
To a woman’s queendom wake !
Round and round !
White and warm and silken-swathed
As a fragrant flower is spathed,
Perfumed tresses lily-crowned,
All in ball-room radiance bathed.
Round and round !
Till the world whirls out of sight,
And the stars dance in the night;
Round my heart a serpent wound,
Long and supple, warm and white.
Round and round!
So the music leads us ever—
As two leaves float down a river.
We are vanquished by the sound.
Closer cling—oh, leave me never !
Round and round !
So I clasp thine hand, and draw
Thy soul homewards by Love’s law.
Till two spirits lost and found,
Grow one soul without a flaw.
Sydney.
NOKOMIS.
The All Nations’ sale at Hordern’s, Pitt-st.,
Sydney, continues with unabated vigour, and the
busy throng that crowds the doors daily is striking
evidence of its growing popularity. Each depart-
ment is replete with the choicest novelties.
Visitors from all nations continue to admire the
products, and the brilliant patriotic emblems of
all nations. Country friends are particularly
invited to take this rare opportunity of selecting
from immense and varied stocks the choice
bargains which will tend to make homes happy.
The God-Fearing Candidate.
Extract from the election-address of a “ God-
fearing ” candidate who proposes standing for
several Australian constituencies at next general
election :
To the Electors. —Gentlemen,—A sacred and
solemn duty lies before you, which can best be
performed by electing me. I can no longer refrain
from coming forward, having received a call
which, as a God-fearing man, I dare not disobey—
viz., the call of the righteous conference of clergy-
men, held in August of last year. A conscious-
ness of the desperate state of the country, and a
knowledge that the demand for God - fearing
candidates is more than equal to the supply,
alone overcomes my modesty. There were
several other men of undoubted character and
probity, whose sterling qualities would certainly
nave caused me toetface myself and humbly support
them as an inferior labourer in the vineyard. They
were, like myself, God-fearing men. But they
are all, unfortunately, dead [and probably their
worst fears are now realised. —Ed. B.] While
thus acknowledging my inferiority to the dead, I
acknowledge no superiors in virtue amongst the
living. I present herewith a short biography and
programme, extracted from the Daily Morning
Howl, and written by the religious editor of that
paper:—
I was born in England or Scotland. No God-
fearing men are ever born elsewhere; and the
native Australian youth are invariably profane
and irreligious. At an early age I attended
Sunday-school in my native village, and subse-
quently taught there. I always touched my hat
to the squire, the parson, and those whom
Providence had placed in authority over us.
During my Sunday-school pupilage I often had
my ears pinched by grown-up teachers of both
sexes. During my later stages as teacher I
pinched in turn the ears, and surreptitiously
smacked the cheeks of the religious youth of Great
Britain. To this treatment of myself I owe my
present position as a God-fearing candidate for
Parliament. I trust my treatment of others has
led to the same happy results. At the age of 29
I had made considerable progress in miscellaneous
piety, was already bald, and shaved my upper-lip.
(The latter characteristic is an unfailing mark of
the pious.) I married a fellow Sunday-school
teacher of the opposite sex, mainly through the
representations of her father and six of her biggest
brothers. This circumstance will show the esteem
in which I was held, and the eagerness with which
an alliance with me was sought. To the loving
ministrations of my wife I owe much. After
being a member of the local Cobden Club, to
which I contributed many of my earliest pennies,
I emigrated to Australia, and started in busi-
ness. Needless to say, I have been very success-
ful. I have been a large employer of labour, and
was the first to introduce the system of com-
mencing the day’s work with prayer (of course,
deducting the time from the men), and I never
employed any but God-fearing men like myself,
whose aspirations after treasure Above led them
to work at reduced wages here Below.
I was elected alderman soon after I became rich
enough to subscribe to all the local charities, race
clubs and church funds. lam a prominent mem-
ber of the Social Gimlet Society, and have a
remarkably quick eye at a keyhole.
In politics I am, of course, a firm believer in
Freetrade and freedom of contract ; in fact, of
absolute freedom in everything—but religion.
I believe in opening the proceedings of Parlia-
ment with prayer, and will do all I can to achieve
that reform. In fact, I believe in opening every-
thing with prayer; and hope that some day in
Possumland nor even an oyster will be opened
without it.
As to taxation, I believe the best interest of the
God-fearing portion of the inhabitants can best
be served by judiciously taxing the other fellow.
I am in favour of a single-tax on land, which I
consider equitable and just, as I do not own any
land myself.
I object to payment of Labour members. Most
of them come from the improvident classes, and
will only spend their salaries on champagne,
caviare and devilled kidneys on toast, leaving
their families unprovided for, and imperilling the
British constitution. None but God-fearing men
should be paid for their services—the value of
which is enhanced by the prayers of the faithful.
I believe in closing up everything but the
churches on Sundays.
lam a strong advocate of flogging and hanging
as a means of suppressing the other fellow. I
should prefer hanging outright, but in these
degenerate times the maudlin sentimentalists have
the upper hand, and we must be content with
small mercies, until the House is composed
entirely of God-fearing men like myself.
If elected, I shall attach myself to the party of
that pure and noble patriot, Sir Sneakurn Sham,
whose venerable grey hairs are a testimony to his
piety, and from whose lips issue at all times the
most noble and elevated sentiments of patriotism
and morality.
I believe in Possumland federating with those
colonies where God-fearing men are at the head of
affairs. I shall oppose to the utmost of my power
any federation with the ungodly colonies of South
Australia and Mapriland.
I am strongly opposed to sweeps, consultations,
and totes, but regularly attend church bazaars,
raffles, and distributions of work,»~oyhich, con-
ducted on God-fearing principles, are a source of
revenue to the Church, and a blessing toman-
kind.
I am also in favour of One-man-one-vote—that
one man to be the man of property.
To those who are alive to the Best Interests of
the Country I need say no more. Living as we
are at a time when the disastrous effects of class
representation are upon us, those to whom I
appeal will give a ready response. Would the
present depression have existed if our statesmen
had commenced their proceedings with earnest
prayer? I cannot think so. What has intimi-
dated the British money-lenders (all God-fearing
men) but the irreligious tendencies of our
colonies? It is nonsense to say that it
was the locking up of =£24,000,000 to prop up the
Baring firm’s Argentine losses, and the conse-
quent withdrawal of that amount from the
investment market. Such assertions can only
carry conviction to the benighted and the un-
godly. When our Legislature is swept of the un-
righteous who now pollute it, and filled with men
who, like myself, are God-fearing, and who will
earn their salaries by prayer alone, then will the
God-fearing British investor yield to us once more
his confidence. As the Book says “ Tis not by
works alone, but by prayer,” &c., so not only
shall we have our Minister of Public Works, but
our Minister of Public Prayer. And, feeling this,
I place myself in the hands of the Almighty and
His electors, and will pray earnestly that their
hearts may be softened towards me.—l am,
gentlemen, yours obediently,
BRIGHAM YOWLMAN.

Missing Word Competition-No. 11.
Any purchaser of The Bulletin who can
correctly supply the word missing from the
paragraph on the subjoined coupon—which
coupon is to be cut out and forwarded to
The Bulletin, accompanied by one shilling,
will be paid the total amount received in
response to this advertisement.
Only one guess is allowed on each Coupon,
but competitors may send as many Coupons
as they please.
Coupons received to Friday, 23rd
February, 1894.
The missing word is placed in a sealed
envelope in the custody of the manager of
the Bank of New Zealand, Sydney, and the
contents are known only to the manager of
this paper.
If the word is not named, the money will
be added to that of the succeeding com-
petition.
The sum of one shilling may be forwarded
by post-office order or stamps of, any pro-
vince ; when the latter are sent, a penny
to be added for exchange.

tralasia and Canada ” as a reward for politicians who have boomed the cause of Imperial
Federation. The brightness of tlie lies in the fact that every other “ colony,
right down to little Montserrat and Walfisch Bay, will be aggrieved because its name
isn’t mentioned in the pompous title of the order, and the end of Imperial federation
will be worse than the beginning.

Directions.
Intending members of this Syndicate should
apply for Shares on form in Schedule B (attached),
but a simple request by letter will do in the first
case. Enclose two addressed envelopes with four-
pence worth of loose stamps for returning Share
Certificate, and to send result of the disposal and
distribution of the properties. Register your
letter, and hold the Post Office receipt for it till
you receive reply from the Promoter. Post Office
Orders and Postal Notes should be crossed, as in
the case of bank cheques. For all replies, loose
stamps should be enclosed. When payment is
made by Cheque or Posta Notes, exchange must
be added with postage stamps to cover it. DO
NOT SEND STAMPS ki payment for Shares;
Postal Notes preferred. If, however, stamps are
sent, please add 5 per cent, exchange. Agents
are not authorised to receive payment for Shares.
